Daytime Crescent Sighting: Consideration for the Coming Night
Clarifies that a daytime crescent sighting is for the next night.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on daytime crescent sighting and explain the evidence from the hadith and the reasoning behind the chosen view.

Core Idea
A daytime crescent sighting signals the coming night, not the past one.

The sighting of the Ramadan crescent is the basis for beginning the fast. But what if the crescent is seen during the day? This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya clarifies that such a sighting is legally considered for the coming night, not the past. The ruling is supported by the hadith 'Fast when you see it' and the understanding of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sighting the crescent during the day. The ruling is that it is not considered for the past night but for the coming night (الليلة المستقبلة).
This applies whether the sighting occurs before or after noon.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith 'صوموا لرؤيته' (Fast when you see it). This is the primary textual evidence.
The hadith is ahad (single-chain) and its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'صوموا' indicates obligation (wujub) according to Hanafi usul, and 'لرؤيته' connects the obligation to the sighting. The Companions and early Muslims understood this to refer to sighting at the evening twilight (عشية), not during the day.
This mutabadir understanding limits the ruling to evening sightings.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf differs: he considers a crescent sighted before noon to be for the past night, affecting both the start and end of Ramadan.
This is an intra-Hanafi disagreement.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The chosen view (المختار) is that daytime sighting is for the coming night, regardless of the time of day. This is the mufta bihi position.
The text quotes al-Kamal and other authorities supporting this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم العظيم لأنّ التواتر لا يبالي فيه بكفر الناقلين فضلاً عن فسقهم أو ضعفهم ذكره الكمال (ولا عبرة برؤية الهلال نهاراً سواء كان قد رؤي قبل الزوال أو ) رؤي (بعده وهو الليلة المستقبلة) لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «صوموا لرؤيته فوجب سبق الرؤية على الصوم والفطر والمفهوم المتبادر منه الرؤية عند عشية كل شهر عند الصحابة والتابعين ومن بعدهم (في المختار) من المذهب .

لزم الصوم بمجرد (الأخبار) حتى لو أخبر رجل عدل القاضي بمجيء رمضان يقبل لغيم، ونحوه، ويأمر الناس بالصوم كذا في الشرح والظاهر أن فيه التفاتاً إلى مذهب الصاحبين القائلين بعدم اشتراط تقدم الدعوى.

قوله : ( في أخبار الجمع العظيم) المراد به ناس كثيرون أخبروا بنحو رؤية الهلال مثلاً، وليس المراد الاثنين إذا رأى القاضي ذلك.

قوله: (ولا عبرة برؤية الهلال نهاراً) أي لا عبرة به من الليلة الماضية بل لليلة المستقبلة.

قوله : (منه) أي منا لحديث.

قوله : (عند عشية كل شهر يعني إذا رأى عند عشية الليل فالليلة الآتية منه، وهذا أنه لها إذا رأى قبل الزوال، وقد ذكره في الدعوى قوله في المختار من المذهب) ويجعل أبو يوسف الهلال المرئي قبل الزوال للماضية في الصوم والفطر وهناك أقوال أخر مذكورة في الشرح والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم.
The Book of Fasting: The great [fasting] because tawatur (widespread transmission) does not care about the disbelief of the transmitters, let alone their sinfulness or weakness; al-Kamal mentioned it. (And no consideration is given to the sighting of the crescent during the day, whether it was sighted before noon or after; it is for the coming night) due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Fast when you see it, so it is obligatory that the sighting precedes the fast and breaking the fast.' The immediately understood meaning from this is the sighting at the evening of every month among the Companions, Successors, and those after them. (In the chosen [view] of the school).

Fasting becomes binding simply upon (the news) such that if a just man informs the judge of the arrival of Ramadan, the judge accepts it due to overcast skies and the like, and orders the people to fast, as stated in the Sharh. And apparently there is a reference to the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who hold that prior litigation is not a condition.

His statement: (Regarding the news of the great multitude) means a large number of people who inform of a crescent sighting, for example; it does not mean two people if the judge sees that.

His statement: (And no consideration is given to the sighting of the crescent during the day) meaning it is not considered for the past night but for the coming night.

His statement: (from it) meaning from us, due to the hadith.

His statement: (at the evening of every month) means if he sees at evening time, the coming night is from it. And this is that if he sees before noon, it is for it, as mentioned in the litigation. His statement: (in the chosen view of the school). Abu Yusuf considers the crescent seen before noon as belonging to the past night regarding fasting and breaking the fast. There are other opinions mentioned in the Sharh. And Allah, the Exalted, knows best. And I seek forgiveness from Allah, the Great.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah is a critical refinement of the ruling concerning the sighting of the crescent moon for Ramadan during the daytime. The author clarifies that if the crescent is sighted during the day, whether before or after noon, it is not considered for the previous night but for the coming night. This means that if someone sees the crescent on the morning of the 30th of Sha'ban, that sighting does not make the previous day the first of Ramadan; rather, it signals that the following night is the beginning of Ramadan. The proof is the hadith 'Fast when you see it' (صوموا لرؤيته), which implies that the sighting occurs at the evening (عشية) of the month, not during the day. The text also notes that Abu Yusuf differs: he considers a sighting before noon to be for the past night in both fasting and breaking the fast. However, the chosen view (المختار) of the Hanafi school is that it is for the coming night. The passage also touches on accepting testimony about the crescent, especially from a multitude, and the significance of widespread transmission (tawatur). This commentary is based solely on the text and includes no external rulings.

Key Terms
عبرة (‘ibrah): Legal consideration; whether a sighting has juridical effect.
الرؤية (al-ru’yah): Sighting of the crescent moon with the naked eye.
المختار (al-mukhtār): The chosen or preferred view within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sees the crescent of Ramadan during the morning of the 30th of Sha'ban, according to the chosen view in this passage, which night does that sighting apply to?
A) The past night (i.e., the previous night is the first of Ramadan)
B) The coming night (i.e., the following night is the first of Ramadan)
C) Neither, the sighting is invalid if during the day
D) Both nights are considered part of Ramadan

Key Takeaway
A daytime crescent sighting is only relevant for the coming night, a ruling rooted in the hadith and the understanding of the early community.

Forgetful Eating, Drinking, and Sex During Fasting
Details that the fast is not broken by forgetful eating, drinking, or sex.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a fasting person eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully, and explain the conditions of withdrawal, qada, and kaffarah upon remembrance.

Core Idea
Forgetful eating does not break the fast; the hadith declares it provision, no qada needed, but immediate withdrawal upon remembrance is wajib.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah clarifies a crucial exception in fasting: forgetfulness. While intentional acts break the fast, forgetful acts do not—provided one stops upon remembering. The text also explores a fiqh etiquette: whether to remind a forgetful fasting person. Let's walk through the ruling, its proof, and the different opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its basic ruling
Recognize that the act is eating/drinking/intercourse done forgetfully (nasya). The ruling: fast remains valid (sahih), no qada required.
This is the core ruling based on the hadith.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'If the fasting person eats forgetfully, it is provision Allah sent to him; no qada upon him.' Intercourse is analogous.
The hadith is ahad (zanni thubut) but accepted as evidence.
3
Trace the condition upon remembrance
If the person remembers during the act, they must withdraw immediately (نزع من فوره). If they continue, the fast is broken.
This distinguishes forgetful from willful acts.
4
Note the consequences of deliberate violation
If after remembrance the person moves without withdrawing or reinserts, kaffarah becomes obligatory. Withdrawal fearing dawn and then emitting after dawn does not incur kaffarah.
Kaffarah only for deliberate intercourse-related violations.
5
Examine the ikhtilaf on reminding
If the forgetful person is strong enough to complete the fast, it is disliked not to remind him. If weak, it is better not to remind. The chosen view distinguishes based on ability.
The dispute is not on the basic ruling but on the obligation of reminding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في بيان ما لا يفسد الصوم وهو أربعة وعشرون شيئاً تقريباً لا تحديداً بالمرة منها ما لو أكل الصائم أو شرب أو جامع أو جمع بينهما ناسياً لصومه لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : إذا أكل الصائم ناسياً فإنما هو رزق ساقه الله إليه فلا قضاء عليه والجماع في معناهما فإن تذكر نزع من فوره فإن مكث بعده فسد صومه فإن حرك نفسه، ولم ينزع ، أو نزع ثم أولج لزمته الكفارة ولو نزع خشية طلوع الفجر فأمنى بعد الفجر والنزع ليس عليه شيء لعدم الجماع صورة ومعنى

وإن كان للناسي قدرة على إتمام الصوم إلى الليل بلا مشقة ظاهرة كشاب قوي يذكره به من راه يأكل و) إن تركه كره عدم تذكيره في المختار كذا في الفتح وقيل من رأى غيره في رمضان يأكل ناسياً لا يخبره لأن بأكله هذا لا يفسد صومه وإذا ذكر الناسي وهو يأكل فقيل له إنك صائم فلم يتذكر يلزمه القضاء في المختار وإن لم يكن له قوة فالأولى عدم تذكيره لما فيه من قطع الرزق واللطف به سواء كان شيخاً، أو شاباً
Chapter on explaining what does not break the fast. It is approximately twenty-four things, not exactly limited. From them: if the fasting person eats or drinks or has intercourse or combines them forgetfully due to his fast, because of the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'If the fasting person eats forgetfully, it is only a provision that Allah has sent to him; there is no qada upon him.' Intercourse is in the same meaning. Then if he remembers, he should withdraw immediately. If he stays after remembrance, his fast is broken. If he moves himself and does not withdraw, or withdraws and then reinserts, the kaffarah becomes obligatory. If he withdraws fearing dawn and then emits after dawn while withdrawing, nothing is upon him due to the absence of intercourse in form and meaning.

And if the forgetful person has the ability to complete the fast until night without apparent hardship, like a strong young man, he should be reminded by the one who sees him eating. And if he leaves him, it is disliked (karahah) not to remind him according to the chosen view, as in al-Fath. It is said that one who sees another eating forgetfully in Ramadan should not inform him, because his eating does not break his fast. If the forgetful person is reminded while eating and is told 'You are fasting' and he does not remember, he is liable for qada according to the chosen view. If he does not have strength, then it is better not to remind him because of cutting off provision and showing kindness to him, whether he is old or young.
Source pages p. 657

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi begins the chapter on what does not break the fast (باب في بيان ما لا يفسد الصوم). The author states that there are approximately twenty-four things, but the exact number is not fixed. He then focuses on the first item: if a fasting person eats, drinks, or has sexual intercourse forgetfully (ناسياً), his fast remains valid and no qada (make-up) is required. The proof is the hadith 'If the fasting person eats forgetfully, it is only a provision that Allah has sent to him; there is no qada upon him.' The same applies to intercourse. However, if during the act the person remembers his fast, he must immediately withdraw; if he continues after remembering, his fast is broken. If he deliberately moves or reinserts, kaffarah (expiation) becomes obligatory. If he withdraws fearing the break of dawn and then emits after dawn, no expiation is required because there was no actual intercourse in form or meaning.

The author then discusses the obligation of reminding a forgetful person. If the forgetful person is capable of completing the fast easily (e.g., a strong young man), the one who sees him eating should remind him. If he does not remind, it is disliked (karahah) according to the chosen view. Some scholars say he should not inform him because his eating does not break his fast. If the forgetful person is reminded and told 'You are fasting' but he does not remember, then qada becomes obligatory according to the chosen view. If the person is weak, it is better not to remind him out of kindness and to avoid cutting off his provision. This commentary is based solely on the text.

Key Terms
ناسياً (nāsiyan): Being forgetful of one's fast at the time of eating, drinking, or intercourse; the opposite of intentional.
القضاء (al-qaḍā): Making up a missed fast on a later day.
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation for deliberately breaking the fast, typically by freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a fasting person eats forgetfully and remembers during the act, what should he do immediately?
A) Continue eating since he already started
B) Withdraw from the food immediately
C) Spit out the food and continue fasting
D) No need to do anything

Key Takeaway
Forgetful eating, drinking, or intercourse does not break the fast, but immediate withdrawal is required upon remembrance; otherwise, the fast breaks and kaffarah may apply.

Ejaculation from Looking or Thinking During Fasting
Rules that ejaculation from looking or thinking does not break the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ejaculation from looking or thinking during fasting and distinguish it from ejaculation caused by physical touch or masturbation.

Core Idea
Looking or thinking causing ejaculation does not break the fast; only physical contact does.

The validity of fasting can be affected by various actions, including ejaculation. This passage clarifies the Hanafi position that not all causes of ejaculation break the fast. The key distinction lies in the presence or absence of direct physical stimulation, which is either the form (insertion) or the meaning (touch with heat) of intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on ejaculation from looking/thinking
The text states clearly that ejaculation from looking at a woman's private part or from mere thought does not break the fast, even if continued deliberately.
2
Extract the legal rationale (ta'lil)
The reason given: because there is neither the physical form (insertion) nor the legal meaning (direct heat/touch) of intercourse present. This forms the core principle.
Understand the distinction between form (surah) and meaning (ma'na).
3
Contrast with ejaculation from touch
Ejaculation from physical touch, even with a barrier that transmits heat, does break the fast. This shows that the 'meaning' (direct stimulation) is present through heat transfer.
This contrast clarifies the boundary of the principle.
4
Identify the additional ruling on masturbation
Masturbation by hand breaks the fast according to the majority of later scholars (عامة المشايخ), and this is the chosen view (المختار). This is a separate rule based on later fatwa authority.
Note the shift in authority from earlier texts to later consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أنزل بنظر إلى فرج امرأة لم يفسد أو فكر وإن أدام النظر والفكر حتى أنزل لأنه لم يوجد منه صورة الجماع، ولا معناه قيد بالنظر لأنّ الإنزال بالمس، ولو بحائل توجد معه الحرارة مفسد، ولو استمنى بكفه فعامة المشايخ أفتوا بفساد الصوم، وهو المختار
Or if he ejaculates by looking at a woman's private part, it does not break the fast. Or by thought, even if he continues looking and thinking until ejaculation, because there is found from him neither the form nor the meaning of intercourse. It is restricted to looking because ejaculation by touch, even with a barrier that creates heat, breaks the fast. And if he masturbates with his hand, the general majority of scholars (عامة المشايخ) have given a fatwa that it breaks the fast, and it is the chosen view.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter on what does not break the fast. The author rules that if a fasting person ejaculates merely by looking at a woman's private part or by thinking (fantasizing), even if he continues looking or thinking until ejaculation, the fast is not broken. The reason (ta'lil) given is that there is present neither the form (صورة) of intercourse (i.e., insertion) nor its meaning (معنى) (i.e., the direct physical stimulation). However, the author qualifies this by contrasting it with ejaculation caused by physical touch (المس), even if with a barrier that transmits heat—that does break the fast. Similarly, masturbation by hand (الاستمناء بالكف) is considered to break the fast according to the majority of later scholars (عامة المشايخ), and this is the chosen view (المختار). The passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the base text, clarifying the conditions under which ejaculation affects the validity of the fast.

Key Terms
الإنزال (al-inzāl): Ejaculation of semen.
صورة الجماع (ṣūrat al-jimā‘): The physical form of intercourse, i.e., insertion.
معنى الجماع (ma‘nā al-jimā‘): The legal meaning or effect of intercourse, i.e., the direct stimulation that typically leads to ejaculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following causes of ejaculation does NOT break the fast?
A) Ejaculation from touching a woman's hand
B) Ejaculation from looking at a woman's private part
C) Ejaculation from masturbating with the hand
D) Ejaculation from intercourse without insertion

Key Takeaway
Ejaculation from non-physical causes (looking, thinking) does not break the fast; only physical stimulation (touch with heat or masturbation) does.

Forgetfulness in Fasting: The Ruling and Its Exceptions
Ruling on forgetful fasting with proof and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating/drinking/intercourse during Ramadan, differentiate between the various scenarios after remembering, and apply the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness excuses the fast; deliberate continuation after remembering triggers kaffara.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi presents the Hanafi ruling on a common scenario: a fasting person who forgets and eats, drinks, or has intercourse. While the basic rule is merciful—no qada or kaffara—the details become critical when the person remembers mid-act. Understanding these nuances prevents common errors in fiqh application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and basic ruling
The passage states that if a fasting person forgets and eats, drinks, or has intercourse, there is no qada and no kaffara. The fast remains valid.
This is based on the hadith: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no qada and no kaffara.'
2
Note the condition of immediate withdrawal
If the person remembers while engaged in the act, he must withdraw immediately (naz'a min fawrihi). This is an obligation.
Failure to withdraw immediately (i.e., staying) spoils the fast but does not yet trigger kaffara.
3
Distinguish between staying and active movement
If he stays after remembering (mukuth), the fast becomes fasid (spoiled), and qada is required, but no kaffara. If he then moves himself (harraka nafsahu) without withdrawing, or withdraws and re-enters, kaffara becomes obligatory.
Active movement (haraka) after remembering is a deliberate act that triggers kaffara.
4
Examine the special case of withdrawal before dawn
If a person withdraws from intercourse fearing dawn, and then ejaculates after dawn, there is no kaffara because there was no intercourse in form or meaning.
The ejaculation occurred outside the actual act of intercourse, so it does not break the fast with kaffara.
5
Understand the distinction between rights of Allah and rights of humans
The commentary notes that forgetfulness is an excuse in matters of worship (rights of Allah) but not in matters of liability for others (rights of humans).
This explains why the forgetfulness exemption is specific to fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ساقه الله إليه فلا قضاء عليه والجماع في معناهما فإن تذكر نزع من فوره فإن مكث بعده فسد صومه فإن حرك نفسه، ولم ينزع ، أو نزع ثم أولج لزمته الكفارة ولو نزع خشية طلوع الفجر فأمنى بعد الفجر والنزع ليس عليه شيء لعدم الجماع صورة ومعنى قوله : (والجماع في معناهما) لأنه من شهوة البطن كالأكل والشرب وأخرج الحاكم من حديث أبي هريرة أنه قال: «من أفطر في رمضان ناسياً فلا قضاء عليه ولا كفارة ا هـ وهو عام في الأكل والشرب والجماع نهر. قوله: (نزع) من فوره أي افتراضاً قوله : (فسد صومه) أي من غير كفارة. قوله: (فإن حرك نفسه الخ) جزم فيه بوجوب الكفارة، وهو الذي في الدر والذي في النهر عن الخلاصة حكايته بقيل، وهو الذي في الفتح أيضاً قوله : (الزمته الكفارة) أنزل أم لا. قوله : (والنزع) لا حاجة إلى ذكره قوله : (لعدم الجماع صورة ومعنى لأن الموجود حال الصوم الإنزال خارج المحل، متذكر لأكله وشربه وجماعة كذا في الشرح وليس النسيان عذراً في حقوق العباد حتى لو أودع وديعة، أو استعار شيئاً فوضعه في محل ونسيه لزمه ضمانه
If Allah causes him to forget, there is no qada upon him. Intercourse is the same as eating and drinking. If he remembers, he must withdraw immediately; if he stays after remembering, his fast is spoiled. If he moves himself and does not withdraw, or withdraws and then enters, the kaffara becomes obligatory. If he withdraws fearing dawn and then ejaculates after dawn, there is nothing upon him due to the absence of intercourse in form and meaning. Commentary: 'Intercourse is the same' because it is from the desire of the belly like eating and drinking. Al-Hakim narrated from Abu Huraira that he said: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no qada upon him and no kaffara.' This is general for eating, drinking, and intercourse. 'Withdraw' means immediately, i.e., as an obligation. 'His fast is spoiled' means without kaffara. 'If he moves himself' is explicitly stated for obligation of kaffara. 'Kaffara becomes obligatory' whether he ejaculates or not. 'Withdraw' is unnecessary to mention. 'Due to absence of intercourse in form and meaning' because what occurs while fasting is ejaculation outside the place. He is mindful of his eating, drinking, and intercourse as stated in the sharh. Forgetfulness is not an excuse in the rights of people; even if he deposits a trust or borrows something and places it somewhere and forgets, he is liable for it.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary on the forgetful fasting person. The core ruling is that a fasting person who forgets and eats, drinks, or has intercourse does not incur any qada or kaffara, because the act was unintentional. However, if he remembers while doing the act, he must immediately stop. If he continues after remembering, his fast is spoiled and qada is required but not kaffara. If he actively engages after remembering (e.g., moves himself without withdrawing), or withdraws and then re-enters, then kaffara becomes obligatory. Special case: if he withdraws before dawn fearing its arrival and then ejaculates after dawn, no kaffara because there was no actual intercourse in form or meaning. The commentary clarifies that 'intercourse is the same' as eating/drinking because all are driven by the desire of the belly. A hadith from Abu Huraira is cited: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no qada nor kaffara.' This hadith generalizes to all three acts. The commentator also distinguishes between the rights of Allah and the rights of humans: forgetfulness is an excuse in matters of worship (rights of Allah), but not in matters of liability for others (rights of humans).

Key Terms
ناسي (nāsī): A person who forgets he is fasting and eats, drinks, or has intercourse.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast; in this context, it is not required for forgetful acts unless he moves himself after remembering.
نزع (naz'): Immediate withdrawal from the act upon remembering; required to maintain validity of fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, what is the ruling for a fasting person who breaks his fast forgetfully?
A) He must make qada but no kaffara.
B) He must make kaffara but no qada.
C) He must make both qada and kaffara.
D) He must make neither qada nor kaffara.

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness during a Ramadan fast excuses the act; the fast remains valid and no qada or kaffara is required, but immediate withdrawal is essential upon remembering.

Reminding a Forgetful Fasting Person: Wajib or Mercy?
Ruling on reminding a forgetful fasting person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings for reminding a strong versus a weak forgetful fasting person and apply the Hanafi mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A strong faster must be reminded; a weak faster should be left alone out of mercy.

When you see someone eating during Ramadan out of forgetfulness, what should you do? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer depending on the person’s strength. This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains the ruling and the reasoning behind it, including an intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between a strong fasting person (e.g., a healthy young man) who can easily complete the fast, and a weak person (elderly or sick) who may struggle. Identify which scenario is being discussed.
2
Determine the hukm for each scenario
For a strong person, reminding is wajib (obligatory); failing to remind is makruh tahrimi. For a weak person, it is better (sunnah) not to remind. State the hukm level for each.
3
Understand the ikhtilaf
Note that there is a qil (some said) that one should never inform a forgetful person because his fast is valid. Recognize that the mufta bihi is the position that distinguishes based on strength.
4
Apply the tarjih (preferred view)
The passage cites al-Fath and al-Tahtawi to affirm the mukhtar view. Understand that the opinion of the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that if the person is reminded but continues, qada is required.
5
Evaluate the ruling on qada
If a strong person is reminded but does not remember and continues eating, qada becomes wajib. This is based on the authority of a single just person's report in religious matters. Consider if kaffara is required (apparently not).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان للناسي قدرة على إتمام (الصوم) إلى الليل بلا مشقة ظاهرة كشاب قوي يذكره به من راه يأكل و) إن تركه كره عدم تذكيره في المختار كذا في الفتح وقيل من رأى غيره في رمضان يأكل ناسياً لا يخبره لأن بأكله هذا لا يفسد صومه وإذا ذكر الناسي وهو يأكل فقيل له إنك صائم فلم يتذكر يلزمه القضاء في المختار وإن لم يكن له قوة فالأولى عدم تذكيره) لما فيه من قطع الرزق واللطف به سواء كان شيخاً، أو شاباً قوله : (يذكره) أي لزوماً كما قال الولوالجي : قال في تحفة الأخيار: ومثله النائم عن الوقت لكن الناسي، أو النائم غير قادر فسقط الإثم عنهما، ووجب على من لم يعلم حالهما تذكير الناسي وإيقاظ النائم إلا في حق الضعيف مرحمة له ا هـ أما إذا علم حاله ففيه التفصيل. قوله : (كره) أي تحريماً. قوله: (لا يخبره) أي مطلقاً قوله : (لأن بأكله فيه حذف اسم أن قوله: (فلم يتذكر) أي بل استمر ثم تذكر يلزمه القضاء عند الشيخين وهو الصحيح لما أنه أخبر بأن الأكل حرام، وخير الواحد حجة في الديانات نهر، ومحله إذا سمع ولم يقع في قلبه صدق أخباره أما إذا لم يسمع فهو في حكم الناسي، فيما يظهر، ولم يتكلموا على حكم الكفارة، والظاهرة عدم وجوبها لعدم تفاحش الجناية بعدم التذكر، ولأن ابتداء الأكل كان ناسياً، وحرره نقلاً. قوله: فالأولى عدم تذكيره عبارة الفتح وسعه أن لا يخبره قوله : لما فيه) أي في التذكير. قوله : (واللطف) عطف على الرزق.
And if the forgetful person has the ability to complete the fast until night without apparent difficulty, like a strong young man, then whoever sees him eating should remind him. If he fails to remind, it is disliked (makruh tahrimi) not to remind him according to the mukhtar. It is reported that whoever sees another forgetting and eating in Ramadan should not inform him because his eating does not break his fast. But if he reminds the forgetful person while he is eating, saying 'You are fasting,' and he does not remember, then qada becomes necessary in the mukhtar view. If he has no strength, then it is better not to remind him due to cutting off sustenance and showing kindness, whether he is old or young. Commentary: 'Remind him' means obligatorily. Al-Walwaliji said in Tuhfat al-Akhyar: Similarly, the one who sleeps through the prayer time, but the forgetful or sleeping person is incapable, so sin is removed from them. It is wajib for one who does not know their state to remind the forgetful and wake the sleeping, except for the weak out of mercy. If their state is known, then detail. 'Disliked' means prohibited. 'He should not inform him' means absolutely. 'Because his eating' has the name 'an' elided. 'He does not remember' means he continues then remembers; qada becomes necessary according to the two shaykhs, and this is sahih because he was told that eating is haram, and the report of a single person is authoritative in religious matters. This applies when he hears but the truth does not settle in his heart; if he does not hear, he remains in the ruling of the forgetful person. They did not discuss kaffara, and apparently it is not wajib due to lack of severity of the transgression, and because the beginning of eating was forgetful. 'It is better not to remind' is from al-Fath, and it is permissible for him not to inform. 'Due to' refers to reminding. 'Kindness' is conjuncted to 'sustenance'.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage discusses the etiquette of reminding a forgetful fasting person. The ruling distinguishes between a person who is strong and can easily complete the fast and one who is weak. If the person is strong (e.g., a young healthy person), it is wajib to remind him; if one fails to do so, it is makruh tahrimi. However, a different opinion states that one should not inform him at all because the forgetful person's fast is still valid. But the mukhtar position is that if after being reminded he still does not remember and continues eating, then qada is obligatory (based on the principle that the testimony of a single just person is authoritative in religious matters). If the forgetful person is weak (elderly, sick), it is better not to remind him out of mercy and to avoid hardship. The commentary further explains that the reminder is obligatory only for those who do not know the person's state. The weak are excluded so as not to cause them difficulty. The ruling focuses on the rights of Allah (forgetfulness is excused) vs. rights of people (the one who sees him has a duty to remind in some cases).

Key Terms
تذكير (tadkīr): Reminding a forgetful fasting person that he is fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it prohibited (makruh tahrimi) to omit reminding a forgetful fasting person?
A) When the person is strong and healthy.
B) When the person is weak and elderly.
C) When the person is eating in private.
D) It is never prohibited to omit reminding.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between a strong faster (must be reminded) and a weak faster (should be left alone), balancing the duty of commanding good with mercy.

What Enters Through Pores Does Not Break the Fast
Ruling on oil, kohl, and eye drops with proof from Aisha's practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that substances entering through pores (masam) do not invalidate the fast to the specific cases of kohl, oil, and eye drops.

Core Idea
What enters through pores does not break the fast – only direct intake through the throat or intercourse nullifies.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi distinction: not every substance that enters the body breaks the fast. The school differentiates between entry through open passages (throat, genitals) and entry through pores (masam). We will examine the evidence from the practice of Aisha and its application to oil, kohl, and eye drops.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Recognize that the Hanafi school holds that entry through pores does not invalidate the fast. This is based on the report of Aisha applying kohl while fasting and the lack of a direct passage to the brain.
The principle is derived from the hadith and qiyas on bathing.
2
Examine the ruling on kohl and oil
The text states that applying oil or kohl does not break the fast, even if the taste or color reaches the throat or saliva. This applies whether the substance is fragrant or not.
The commentary clarifies that what enters through pores is not considered a nullifier.
3
Understand the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Aisha applying kohl while fasting, which indicates approval. Also, the anatomical fact that there is no passage between the eye and the brain.
The dalil is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but the ruling is strengthened by majority agreement.
4
Distinguish from the Shafi'i view
The Shafi'i school holds that if the taste of kohl reaches the throat, it breaks the fast. The Hanafi view is that only entry through the throat directly (like swallowing) matters, not through pores.
This is a well-known ikhtilaf.

Source Text

Applying oil does not break the fast, just as if he bathed and felt the coolness of water in his liver. Or applying kohl, even if he finds its taste in his throat, or its color in his saliva or phlegm, according to the sahih position of the majority, whether it is fragrant or not. The issue of kohl and oil for the mustache is restricted: it is not disliked for a fasting person to smell the scent of musk, rose, and similar substances that are not a connected physical particle, like smoke, for they said kohl is not disliked under any condition, including fragrant types. 'Prohibition does not necessitate breaking the fast' means the prohibition of persisting in looking. 'Act of two women' means lesbianism without ejaculation; if with ejaculation, qada is required. 'Does not break his fast' due to absence of a nullifier; what enters through pores does not nullify. 'Just as if he bathed' - Imam disliked entering water and wrapping in wet garment because it shows distress in worship, not because it resembles breaking fast. 'Or applied kohl' - based on the report that Aisha applied kohl while fasting; there is no passage between eye and brain, and tears exit by seepage like sweat; what enters through pores does not nullify. 'Or his phlegm' - 'nukhamah' with nun. 'And the restriction' does not imply prohibition; it only negates breakage, not dislike. 'They said' implies no dislike. 'Mustache oil' will come later in the chapter on kaffara. 'Like smoke' exemplifies things that are not physical particles. 'They said' is the cause for the restriction. 'Similarly mustache oil' means they did not restrict it to a type of oil. 'With oil' - preferable to say with kohl. 'If he swallows a grape' - any food that does not crumble. 'If he inserts his finger' - the sharh says similarly if he inserts into his anus, or a woman into her vagina, according to the mukhtar, unless it is wet with water or oil. 'Cupping' - incomplete.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage covers several actions that do not break the fast. First, ejaculation caused by looking or thinking (without direct physical contact) does not invalidate the fast because there is no form or meaning of intercourse. The ruling applies even if he continues looking or thinking deliberately, as the prohibition is separate from the nullification of the fast. Second, lesbianism (two women without penetration) does not break the fast unless ejaculation occurs. Third, applying oil to the body or kohl to the eyes does not break the fast, even if the taste or color reaches the throat or saliva, because the substance enters through pores (masam) and that is not considered as entering through a direct passage. The same applies to using eye drops or milk/medicine with oil. Fourth, swallowing a grape attached to a string and then removing it does not break the fast as long as no part disintegrates. Fifth, inserting a dry finger into the anus or vagina does not break the fast; if wet, it may break. Cupping (hijama) is also mentioned but the text cuts off; it is generally not a nullifier. The commentary cites the report of Aisha applying kohl while fasting. It also clarifies that what enters through pores (like when bathing) is not a nullifier.

Key Terms
مسام (masām): Pores of the body through which substances can enter without breaking the fast.
اكتحال (iktihāl): The act of applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the kohl is not food.
B) Because it enters through pores and there is no direct passage to the brain.
C) Because the Prophet never forbade it.
D) Because it is a minor act.

Key Takeaway
The fast remains valid when substances enter through pores (masam), such as kohl, oil, and eye drops, based on the Hanafi principle and the practice of Aisha.

Ejaculation by Sight or Thought Does Not Break the Fast
Ruling on ejaculation by looking/thinking and lesbianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi fiqh rules that ejaculation caused by looking or thinking does not invalidate the fast, and distinguish it from ejaculation caused by direct contact.

Core Idea
Ejaculation without direct contact does not break the fast – prohibition is separate from nullification.

This lesson addresses a sensitive but practical issue: what if a fasting person experiences ejaculation due to looking at or thinking about sexual matters? The Hanafi ruling is that the fast remains valid because there is no 'form or meaning of intercourse'. However, the act of looking or thinking may be sinful. We will unpack the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text states that ejaculation resulting from looking at a private part or from thinking does not break the fast, even if the looking/thinking is continuous.
The key is that the ejaculation is not a direct result of physical contact.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
The reason given is 'because there is no form or meaning of intercourse'. The 'form' is the physical act of intercourse; the 'meaning' is ejaculation resulting from direct contact (mubasharah). Since neither is present, the fast remains intact.
The text also says: 'Nor does the prohibition of looking entail breaking the fast.'
3
Apply to lesbianism
The same principle applies to two women engaging in sexual acts without ejaculation: it does not break the fast. If ejaculation occurs (from either), then qada is required.
This is a practical example of the same rule.
4
Distinguish from direct contact
If ejaculation occurs due to direct touching (mubasharah), the fast is broken and requires both qada and kaffara. The distinction hinges on the presence or absence of physical contact.
This contrast is important for application.

'Prohibition does not necessitate breaking the fast' means the prohibition of persisting in looking. 'Act of two women' means lesbianism without ejaculation; if with ejaculation, qada is required. 'Does not break his fast' due to absence of a nullifier; what enters through pores does not nullify. 'Just as if he bathed' - Imam disliked entering water and wrapping in wet garment because it shows distress in worship, not because it resembles breaking fast. 'Or applied kohl' - based on the report that Aisha applied kohl while fasting; there is no passage between eye and brain, and tears exit by seepage like sweat; what enters through pores does not nullify. 'Or his phlegm' - 'nukhamah' with nun. 'And the restriction' does not imply prohibition; it only negates breakage, not dislike. 'They said' implies no dislike. 'Mustache oil' will come later in the chapter on kaffara. 'Like smoke' exemplifies things that are not physical particles. 'They said' is the cause for the restriction. 'Similarly mustache oil' means they did not restrict it to a type of oil. 'With oil' - preferable to say with kohl. 'If he swallows a grape' - any food that does not crumble. 'If he inserts his finger' - the sharh says similarly if he inserts into his anus, or a woman into her vagina, according to the mukhtar, unless it is wet with water or oil. 'Cupping' - incomplete.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage covers several actions that do not break the fast. First, ejaculation caused by looking or thinking (without direct physical contact) does not invalidate the fast because there is no form or meaning of intercourse. The ruling applies even if he continues looking or thinking deliberately, as the prohibition is separate from the nullification of the fast. Second, lesbianism (two women without penetration) does not break the fast unless ejaculation occurs. Third, applying oil to the body or kohl to the eyes does not break the fast, even if the taste or color reaches the throat or saliva, because the substance enters through pores (masam) and that is not considered as entering through a direct passage. The same applies to using eye drops or milk/medicine with oil. Fourth, swallowing a grape attached to a string and then removing it does not break the fast as long as no part disintegrates. Fifth, inserting a dry finger into the anus or vagina does not break the fast; if wet, it may break. Cupping (hijama) is also mentioned but the text cuts off; it is generally not a nullifier. The commentary cites the report of Aisha applying kohl while fasting. It also clarifies that what enters through pores (like when bathing) is not a nullifier.

Key Terms
إنزال (inzāl): Ejaculation.
صورة الجماع (ṣūrat al-jimā'): The physical form of intercourse.
معنى الجماع (ma'nā al-jimā'): The meaning/result of intercourse, i.e., ejaculation from direct contact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does ejaculation from thinking not break the fast?
A) Because thinking is not an action.
B) Because there is no direct contact (mubasharah).
C) Because the fast is only broken by food and drink.
D) Because it is difficult to avoid.

Key Takeaway
Ejaculation from looking or thinking does not break the fast because it lacks direct physical contact; the sin of looking is separate from nullification.

Miscellaneous Actions: Grape, Finger, Cupping
Rulings on swallowing a tied grape, finger insertion, and cupping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of non-nullification to actions like swallowing a tied grape, inserting a dry finger, and cupping during fasting.

Core Idea
These actions do not break the fast because they involve no legal consumption or intercourse.

This lesson covers three less common scenarios: swallowing a grape attached to a string, inserting a finger into the anus/vagina, and cupping (hijama). The common thread is that they do not involve the legal definition of eating, drinking, or sexual intercourse. Understanding these edge cases helps solidify the overall framework of what nullifies the fast.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the grape scenario
If a person swallows a grape (or similar) tied to a string and then removes it, the fast is not broken because no part of the food remains inside. The grape was not ingested; it was merely inserted and retrieved.
This applies to any food that can be retrieved intact without dissolving.
2
Examine the finger insertion
Inserting a dry finger into the anus or vagina does not break the fast. The text specifies 'dry' – if the finger is wet with water or oil, it may break the fast because something enters the body.
The commentary adds that the ruling applies to both male and female for the respective orifices.
3
Consider cupping (hijama)
The text mentions cupping but is cut off. The general Hanafi ruling is that cupping does not break the fast, though it is disliked (makruh) because it weakens the body. The proof is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was cupped while fasting.
The OCR cut off, but the ruling is well-known from other sources.
4
Identify the common principle
All these actions do not involve the entry of food or drink into the stomach, nor do they involve sexual intercourse. Even if some substance enters (like in cupping, blood is removed), it does not constitute a nullifier.
The principle is that nullifiers are specific to consumption and intercourse.

Dust, Flies, and Taste of Medicine Do Not Break the Fast
Ruling on unavoidable substances in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on unavoidable substances entering the throat during fasting and explain why the principle of istihsan overrides the general rule.

Core Idea
What cannot be avoided does not invalidate the fast.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a practical concern for fasting Muslims: what if dust from the mill, a fly, or the taste of medicine enters the throat unintentionally? The general rule is that any substance entering the body cavity intentionally breaks the fast. However, the Hanafi school introduces an exception based on the principle of istihsan: if entry is unavoidable, the fast remains valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Entering of dust, flies, medicine taste into throat during fasting). What is the ruling? (Fast remains valid – sahih).
The text clearly states 'so the fast is not spoiled'.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The text gives the reason: 'because it is not possible to guard against them'. This is not a direct textual proof but a rational principle (istihsan).
Istihsan is a secondary source in Hanafi usul, allowing exception from a general ruling for necessity or custom.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this reason prove the ruling? The general rule is that intentional entry invalidates the fast. Unavoidable entry is not intentional; one cannot reasonably avoid it. The principle of istihsan permits an exception.
This is classified as istihsan bi al-urf or istihsan based on necessity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Which school disagrees? The Shafi'i school generally holds that any entry into the belly, even unintentional, breaks the fast if one is mindful of fasting.
The Hanafi position is based on ease and avoidance of hardship.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon and why? The Hanafi position is mufta bihi due to the principle of la yumkin al-ihtiraz (impossibility of guarding). Later Hanafi masters like al-Tahtawi affirm it.
Zahir al-Riwayah also supports this; it is not an isolated opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو دخل حلقه غبار ولو كان غبار دقيق الطاحون أو دخل حلقه ذباب أو دخل أثر طعم الأدوية فيه أي في حلقه لأنه لا يمكن الاحتراز عنها، فلا يفسد الصوم بدخولها وهو ذاكر لصومه لما ذكرنا
Or dust enters his throat, even if it is fine dust of the mill, or a fly enters his throat, or the trace of the taste of medicines enters his throat, because it is not possible to guard against them, so the fast is not spoiled by their entry while he is mindful of his fast due to what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage presents a set of rulings from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi on what does not invalidate the fast when it enters the throat unintentionally. The basis is the principle that only intentional and avoidable entry of substances breaks the fast. Dust, flies, and residual taste of medicine are classified as unavoidable (la yumkin al-ihtiraz), so fasting remains valid even if the person is conscious of fasting. The commentary contrasts this with intentional inhalation of smoke, which breaks the fast because one can avoid it. The ruling relies on qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) to the case of water entering the nose unintentionally after rinsing the mouth. The condition 'while mindful of his fast' (wa huwa dhakirun li-sawmihi) indicates that if one were forgetful, the fast is not broken by consensus.

Key Terms
لا يمكن الاحتراز (la yumkin al-ihtiraz): It is not possible to guard against or avoid the substance entering the throat.
غبار (ghubar): Dust or fine particles that may hang in the air and enter the throat unintentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because dust is considered pure and not a food.
B) Because it is impossible to guard against it.
C) Because the person is not mindful of the fast.
D) Because dust does not reach the stomach.

Key Takeaway
Unavoidable substances like dust, flies, and medicine taste do not invalidate the fast in the Hanafi school due to the principle of istihsan.

Ruling on Unavoidable Substances Entering the Throat During Fasting
States a ruling on unavoidable entry of substances during fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the entry of unavoidable substances into the throat during fasting and identify the condition of unavoidability as the basis for non-nullification.

Core Idea
What cannot be avoided does not break the fast — unavoidability exempts from nullification.

In this short passage from the matn, we encounter a specific ruling related to the fast: if a fly or the taste of medicine enters the throat while one is mindful of the fast, the fast is not broken. The reason given is that such things cannot be avoided (la yumkin al-ihtiraz). This is a practical application of the general principle that unintentional and unavoidable intrusions do not nullify the fast. Let's parse the ruling and its condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the act is the entry of a fly or medicinal taste into the throat while fasting. The ruling is that the fast is not spoiled (sahih).
The key term 'la yumkin al-ihtiraz' is the reason.
2
Examine the condition: unavoidability
The text states 'because it is impossible to guard against them'. This condition exempts the entry from nullification. If the substance could have been avoided, the ruling would differ.
The Hanafi school uses this principle broadly for similar cases.
3
Apply to other scenarios
Consider other unavoidable substances like dust, smoke, or small insects that may enter the mouth unintentionally. The same principle applies.
Be careful to distinguish intentional ingestion.
4
Note the context of mindfulness
The ruling applies when the person is mindful (dhakir) of the fast. If forgetful, the ruling might differ, but here it is explicit.
Mindfulness is a condition for this ruling; see the text 'wa huwa dhakir li-sawmihi'.

Source Text

The book of fasting: (the pollinating [substance?] or) a fly entered his throat, (or) the taste effect of medicine entered it, i.e., his throat, because it is impossible to guard against them, so fasting is not spoiled by their entry, while he is mindful of his fasting due to what we mentioned.

Commentary
This passage from the matn states a clear ruling: if a fly or the taste of medicine enters the throat while one is mindful of the fast, the fast is not broken. The reason given is that such things cannot be completely avoided (la yumkin al-ihtiraz). The hashia points out that 'lima dhakarna' refers to this reason previously stated. This ruling is straightforward with no dispute mentioned.

Key Terms
لا يمكن الاحتراز عنها (la yumkin al-ihtiraz 'anha): Impossibility of guarding against something — a legal principle that exempts from nullification when the substance cannot be avoided.

Janabah Does Not Invalidate the Fast
States that waking up in janabah does not break the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting while in a state of janabah and identify the Quranic and hadith proofs.

Core Idea
Janabah does not break the fast; the fast remains valid even if one remains junub all day.

A common misconception among students is that being in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah) invalidates one's fast. The Hanafi school, however, holds that the fast remains perfectly valid even if one wakes up junub and continues in that state for the entire day. This lesson examines the evidence from the Quran and the Sunnah that establishes this ruling, clarifying the distinction between conditions of ritual purity for prayer and those for fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage and identify the explicit ruling: a person who becomes junub and remains in that state for one or more days still has a valid fast.
2
Locate the primary proof
The passage cites Quran 2:187 (فالآن باشروهن). Understand how this verse implies the ruling: the permission for intercourse until just before dawn necessarily leads to janabah after dawn, and no order is given to cancel the fast.
The Quranic proof is based on the implication of permissibility, not a direct command.
3
Identify the hadith proof
The passage quotes a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) says he wakes up junub, intends to fast, bathes, and fasts. This confirms the practice.
The hadith is explicit and practical.
4
Apply the ruling
Conclude that if one wakes up junub, the fast is valid. Ghusl is required for prayer but not for the validity of the fast itself.
The ruling applies to both Ramadan and voluntary fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أصبح جنباً، ولو استمر على حالته يوماً أو أياماً (بالجنابة) لقوله تعالى : فالآن باشروهن، لاستلزام جواز المباشرة إلى قبيل الفجر وقوع الغسل بعده ضرورة وقوله : «وأنا أصبح جنباً وأنا أريد الصيام وأغتسل وأصوم
Or he becomes junub, even if he continues in that state for a day or multiple days (in janabah) due to His statement: 'So now have sexual relations with them' (Quran 2:187), because the permission of direct contact until just before dawn necessitates the occurrence of bathing after it necessarily, and his statement: 'I wake up junub and I intend fasting, and I bathe and I fast.'
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that a person who wakes up in a state of janabah (major ritual impurity) does not break his fast, even if he remains in that state for one or more days. The proof is from Quran 2:187 which permits intimacy until just before dawn, implying that bathing after that period is inevitable. A hadith is also cited where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said he would wake up junub, intend to fast, bathe, and fast. The hashia clarifies the proof: the Quranic verse establishes the permissibility of intercourse up to the time of pre-dawn, which inevitably leads to the necessity of bathing after subh sadiq. The hadith confirms the practice. This ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): A state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, typically after sexual activity or seminal emission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if one wakes up in a state of janabah and continues the fast?
A) The fast is invalid.
B) The fast is valid but ghusl is obligatory before dawn.
C) The fast is valid and ghusl can be done later.
D) The fast is makruh.

Key Takeaway
A person who wakes up in a state of janabah (major impurity) during a fast has a valid fast and is not required to make it up; ghusl is needed only for prayer.

Pouring Liquid into the Urethra During Fasting
Intra-Hanafi dispute on urethral entry in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pouring liquid into the urethra during fasting, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and identify the basis of the disagreement.

Core Idea
The bladder's status as an internal cavity is medically debated, but the majority rule: fast intact.

This lesson examines a specific case where liquid enters the body through an unusual route: the urethra. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on whether reaching the bladder breaks the fast. The ruling hinges on an anatomical question: is the bladder an internal opening (manfadh al-jawf)? Medical evidence informs the fiqh, showing how the school integrates empirical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling on: pouring water or oil into the urethra. Note the baseline ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: it does not break the fast.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Recognize that Abu Yusuf disagrees when the liquid reaches the bladder. For him, that breaks the fast. Note that there is consensus as long as the liquid stays in the urethra.
The dispute is only about reaching the bladder.
3
Analyze the basis of disagreement
The dispute is based on whether the bladder is considered an internal body cavity (manfadh al-jawf). The text says 'the apparent view is that it is an opening,' yet Abu Hanifa and Muhammad still rule no break. Understand that they rely on medical knowledge: urine collects by filtration, not direct entry, so the bladder is not a direct passage.
The ruling is consistent: even if the bladder is an opening, the mechanism of entry is indirect.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Note that the text presents the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad as the relied-upon position (al-azhar). This is the operative ruling for fatwa, though the disagreement is acknowledged.
Later Hanafi fatwa works may follow this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو صب في إحليله ماء أو دهناً لا يفطر عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد خلافاً لأبي يوسف فيما إذا وصل إلى المثانة أما ما دام في قصبة الذكر لا يفسد بالاتفاق، ومبنى الخلاف على منفذ الجوف من المثانة وعدمه والأظهر أنه منفذ له وإنما يجتمع البول المثانة بالترشيح كذا تقوله الأطباء قاله الزيلعي
Or he poured water or oil into his urethra: it does not break the fast according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, if it reaches the bladder. As long as it remains in the penis, it does not break the fast by agreement. The basis of the disagreement is whether the bladder is an internal opening (manfadh al-jawf) or not. The apparent view is that it is an opening for it, and urine only collects in the bladder by filtration, as the physicians say, mentioned by al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the entry of water or oil into the urethra. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, the fast is not broken unless the substance reaches the bladder (al-mathana). If it stays in the urethral canal, it does not break the fast by consensus. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that even if it reaches the bladder, the fast breaks. The underlying principle is whether the bladder is considered an internal body cavity (manfadh al-jawf). The text notes that the preferred view (al-azhar) is that the bladder is an opening, relying on medical opinion that urine gathers there by filtration. The hashia attributes this to al-Zayla'i. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad unless specified otherwise in later texts.

Key Terms
مثانة (mathana): The bladder; considered by some an internal body cavity, by others not a direct opening.
منفذ الجوف (manfadh al-jawf): An opening to the internal cavity of the body; if a substance reaches such an opening, it may break the fast.
إحليل (ihlil): Urethra; the duct through which urine is discharged.
قصبة الذكر (qasabat al-dhakar): The shaft of the penis; the passage through which urine flows.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, does pouring water into the urethra break the fast if it reaches the bladder?
A) Yes, it breaks the fast.
B) No, it does not break the fast.
C) It breaks only if it is oil.
D) It breaks only if done intentionally.

Key Takeaway
The fast is not broken by liquid in the urethra reaching the bladder according to the majority Hanafi view, based on medical evidence that the bladder is not a direct internal cavity.

Ruling on Swallowing Phlegm During Fasting
Explains the ruling on phlegm and the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing phlegm and identify the relied-upon position in the school.

Core Idea
Swallowing phlegm less than a mouthful never breaks the fast; even a mouthful is excused according to Abu Hanifa's relied-upon view.

The fast is a worship that requires awareness of what nullifies it. One common concern is phlegm (nukhamah) that may be swallowed inadvertently or deliberately. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position, distinguishing between small amounts and mouthfuls, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its quantity
Determine if the phlegm is less than a mouthful or a mouthful.
Consensus exists on less than mouthful.
2
Note the consensus on less than mouthful
All scholars agree that less than a mouthful does not break the fast.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf on a mouthful
Abu Hanifa holds it does not break; Abu Yusuf holds it does.
The relied-upon (mu'tamad) position is Abu Hanifa's.
4
Understand the recommendation to spit
Spitting is advised to avoid disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.
This is a taqwa-based precaution, not an obligation.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case of swallowed phlegm, state the hukm and the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كان أقل من ملء فيه لا ينقض إجماعاً، وإن كان ملء فيه ينقض صومه عند أبي يوسف، وعند أبي حنيفة لا ينقض ( وينبغي إلقاء النخامة حتى لا يفسد صومه على قول الإمام الشافعي) كما نبه عليه العلامة ابن الشحنة ليكون صومه صحيحاً بالاتفاق لقدرته على مجها (أو ذرعه) أي سبقه وغلبه (القيء) ولو ملا فاه لقوله : من ذرعه القيء وهو صائم فليس عليه القضاء، وإن استقاء عمداً فليقض . (و) كذا لا يفطر لو (عاد) ما ذرعه (بغير صنعه ولو ملا) القيء (فمه في الصحيح) وهذا عند محمد لأنه لم يوجد صورة الفطر وهو الابتلاع ولا معناه لأنه لا يتغذى به عادة (أو استقاء) أي تعمد إخراجه، وكان أقل من ملء فمه على الصحيح) وهذا عند أبي يوسف وقال محمد يفسد وهو ظاهر الرواية (ولو أعاده في الصحيح) لا يفسد عند أبي يوسف كما في المحيط لعدم الخروج حكماً، ولا ينقض الطهارة وقال الكمال وهو المختار عند بعضهم لعدم الخروج شرعاً، وقال محمد: يفسد وهو ظاهر الرواية ورواية عن أبي يوسف لإطلاق ما روينا أو أكل ما بین أسنانه) مما بقي فيه من سحوره وكان دون الحمصة لأنه تبع لريقه، وهذا القدر لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه عادة، أو وقال الكمال من المشايخ من جعل الفاصل بين االقليل والكثير ما يحتاج في ابتلاعه إلى الإستعانة بالريق، أو لا يحتاج الأول قليل، والثاني كثير وهو حسن يتعسر لأن ما لو جمع الريق قصداً ثم ابتلعه فإنه لا يفسد صومه في أصح الوجهين كما في المنح. قوله: (وعند أبي حنيفة لا ينقض ) هو المعتمد قوله : (حتى لا يفسد صومه) حتى تفريعية والفعل بعدها مرفوع قوله : ( لقدرته على مجها علة لقوله : وينبغي الخ. قوله : (ولا معناه) أي المقصود منه، وهو التغذي. قوله : (أو استقاء الحاصل كما في شرح السيد أن جملة المسائل اثنتا عشرة لأنه أما أن يكون قاء، أوا ستقاء، وكل أما أن يكون ملء الفم، أو دونه، وكل من الأربعة أما أن يكون عاد بنفسه، أو أعاده أو خرج ولا يفطر في الكل على الأصح إلا في الإعادة والاستقاء بشرط ملء الفم، ولو استقاء مراراً في مجلس ملء الفم أفطر لا إن كان في مجالس، أو غدوة، ثم نصف النهار ثم عشية وهذا على قول الثاني. قوله : (الإطلاق ما روينا) من قوله : وإن استقاء عمداً فليقض». قوله : (من سحوره) بفتح السين. قوله: (وكان دون الحمصة سواء ابتلعه أو مضغه وسواء قصد ابتلاعه أم لا، كما في النهر، وهذا هو المشهور، وفي خزانة الأكمل المفسد ما يزيد على قدر الحمصة نقله السيد، والحمصة بكسر الحاء. وتشديد الميم مفتوحة ومكسورة . قوله : (الأول) (قليل كذا في الشرح والصواب عكس العبارة، ويدل عليه ما في شرح السيد حيث قال : وقال الدبوسي : هذا للتقريب، والتحقيق أن الكثير ما يحتاج في ابتلاعه إلى الاستعانة بالريق واستحسنه في الفتح ا هـ، ونحوه في النهر. قوله: (وذلك) أي عدم سهولة الاحتراز . المانع من الحكم بالإفطار بعد تحقق الوصول كونه لا يسهل الاحتراز عنه وذلك مما يجري بنفسه مع الريق لا فيما يتعمد في إدخاله لأنه غير مضطر فيه انتهى (أو مضغ مثل سمسمة) أي قدرها وقد تناولها من خارج فمه حتى تلاشت ولم يجد لها طعماً في حلقه) كذا الكافي، وقال الكمال، وهذا حسن جداً فليكن الأصل في كل قليل مضغه انتهى . قوله: (أو مضغ مثل سمسمة) قيد بالمضغ لأنه لو ابتلعها يفسد صومه، وفي وجوب الكفارة قولان مصححان ذكره السيد قوله: (وهذا) أي اعتبار وجود الطعم في الحلق، وعدمه. قوله : ( فليكن) أي وجود الطعم في الحلق وعدمه الأصل أي الضابط في كل قليل مضغه والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم واستغفر الله العظيم.
If it is less than a mouthful, it does not break the fast by consensus. If it is a mouthful, it breaks his fast according to Abu Yusuf, and according to Abu Hanifa it does not break. (And it is recommended to spit out the phlegm so that his fast is not invalidated according to Imam al-Shafi'i) as al-Allama Ibn al-Shihna noted, so that his fast is valid by agreement because he is able to expel it. (Or if vomiting overcomes him) i.e., it precedes and overwhelms him, even if it fills his mouth, due to his saying: 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting while fasting has no make-up upon him, but whoever induces vomiting intentionally must make it up.' (And) similarly, it does not break the fast if (he returns) what overcame him (without his action, even if it fills) the vomit (his mouth, according to the sound opinion) This is according to Muhammad because the form of breaking the fast, which is swallowing, did not occur, nor its meaning because one does not normally nourish by it. (Or he induces vomiting) i.e., intentionally expels it, and it is less than a mouthful, according to the sound opinion) This is according to Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad said it invalidates, and that is the apparent narration. (And if he returns it in the sound opinion) it does not invalidate according to Abu Yusuf as in al-Muhit, because there is no exit in the legal sense, and it does not break purity. Al-Kamal said it is the chosen view according to some, because there is no exit legally. Muhammad said it invalidates, and that is the apparent narration and a narration from Abu Yusuf, due to the general import of what we narrated, or he eats what is between his teeth from his suhur remnants and it is less than a chickpea because it follows his saliva, and this amount cannot normally be avoided. Or al-Kamal said that some scholars made the distinction between little and much: what requires assistance of saliva to swallow, or not; the first is little, the second is much, and it is good. It is difficult because if one intentionally collects saliva then swallows it, it does not invalidate his fast in the most correct of the two views as in al-Minh. His saying: (And according to Abu Hanifa it does not break) is the relied-upon. His saying: (so that his fast is not invalidated) 'حتى' is a consequential particle and the verb after it is marfu'. His saying: (because he is able to expel it) is the reason for his saying: 'and it is recommended' etc. His saying: (and not its meaning) i.e., the intended purpose of it, which is nourishment. His saying: (or induces vomiting) the outcome, as in Sharh al-Sayyid, that the total cases are twelve: because either he vomits or induces vomiting, and each either fills the mouth or less, and each of the four either returns by itself or he returns it or it comes out, and fasting is not broken in all according to the soundest except in returning and inducing vomiting with the condition of filling the mouth. And if he induces vomiting multiple times in one session filling the mouth, he breaks his fast, not if in separate sessions, or morning then noon then evening, and this according to the second. His saying: (the general import of what we narrated) from his saying: 'And whoever induces vomiting intentionally must make it up.' His saying: (from his suhur) with fatha on the sin. His saying: (and it is less than a chickpea, whether he swallows it or chews it, and whether he intends to swallow it or not, as in al-Nahr, and this is the well-known view. And in Khizanat al-Akmal, what invalidates is what exceeds the size of a chickpea, as al-Sayyid transmitted. And 'chickpea' with kasr on the ha and tashdid on the mim, open and broken. His saying: (the first) (little) thus in the sharh, but the correct is the opposite of the phrase, and it is indicated by what is in Sharh al-Sayyid where he said: al-Dabusi said: this is for approximation, and the verification is that much is what needs assistance of saliva to swallow, and it is approved in al-Fath, etc., and similar in al-Nahr. His saying: (and that) i.e., the lack of ease of avoidance. The obstacle to ruling of breaking the fast after confirming arrival is that it is not easy to avoid, and that is what flows by itself with saliva, not in what is intentionally introduced, because he is not forced to do it. End. (Or he chews something like a sesame seed) i.e., its size, and he took it from outside his mouth until it dissolved and he did not find its taste in his throat.' Thus in al-Kafi. And al-Kamal said, this is very good, so let the principle in every chewed little thing be that. End. His saying: (or chews something like a sesame seed) is restricted to chewing because if he swallows it, it invalidates his fast, and regarding the obligation of expiation, there are two sound opinions mentioned by al-Sayyid. His saying: (and this) i.e., consideration of the presence of taste in the throat or its absence. His saying: (so let) i.e., the presence of taste in the throat or its absence is the principle, i.e., the criterion in every chewed little thing. And Allah, exalted and glorified, knows best, and I seek forgiveness of Allah the Almighty.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on Maraqi al-Falah, dealing with rulings on what does not break the fast. The commentator explains nuanced cases involving phlegm, vomiting, and food remnants. For phlegm (nukhamah): less than a mouthful does not break the fast by consensus. A mouthful breaks according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Abu Hanifa—the latter being the relied-upon position. To avoid disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, one should spit it out. For vomiting: if overpowering (dhar'ahu), no make-up required, based on the hadith; if intentionally induced, make-up is required. If vomit returns by itself, fasting is not broken, even if it fills the mouth, according to Muhammad. Induced vomiting less than a mouthful does not break according to Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad says it invalidates (the apparent narration). Returning induced vomit: has same ikhtilaf. For remnants between teeth: less than a chickpea does not break, as it follows saliva and is hard to avoid. The distinction between little and much: little is what can be swallowed without aid of saliva; much requires aid. Regarding chewing a sesame-sized object from outside: if chewed and no taste reaches throat, the fast is valid—the principle is taste in the throat. The commentary also summarizes twelve cases of vomiting/inducing based on quantity and self-return/reintroduction.

Key Terms
النخامة (al-nukhamah): Phlegm or mucus that comes from the chest or nose

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, swallowing phlegm that fills the mouth during fasting:
A) breaks the fast
B) does not break the fast
C) breaks the fast and requires expiation
D) is makruh and breaks the fast

Key Takeaway
Swallowing phlegm, even a mouthful, does not break the fast according to the operative Hanafi position.

Rulings on Involuntary and Induced Vomiting During Fasting
Explains the rulings on vomiting with hadith evidence and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between overpowering and intentional vomiting, state the ruling for each, and apply the Hanafi position on returning vomit.

Core Idea
Overpowering vomiting does not break the fast; intentional vomiting does, but returning vomit without action does not invalidate.

Vomiting is a common occurrence that raises questions about its effect on fasting. The hadith explicitly differentiates between vomiting that overcomes a person and vomiting that is intentionally induced. This lesson unpacks the rulings, including the nuances of returning vomit and the twelve-case summary provided by later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of vomiting
Determine if the vomiting was overpowering (dhar'ahu) or intentionally induced (istaqa'a).
The hadith is the primary evidence.
2
Apply the hadith ruling
Overpowering: no qada'. Intentional: qada' required.
3
Consider the quantity
If the vomit fills the mouth, ruling may differ for intentional vomiting.
Induced vomiting less than mouthful: Abu Yusuf says no break; Muhammad says break.
4
Analyze returning vomit
If vomit returns on its own: no break. If one returns it intentionally: ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
Mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf: does not break.
5
Apply the twelve-case synthesis
The text summarizes twelve possibilities; most do not break except intentional vomiting a mouthful and returning it.
