اعتبار وجود الطعم في الحلق، وعدمه. قوله : ( فليكن) أي وجود الطعم في الحلق وعدمه الأصل أي الضابط في كل قليل مضغه والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم واستغفر الله العظيم.
If it is less than a mouthful, it does not break the fast by consensus. If it is a mouthful, it breaks his fast according to Abu Yusuf, and according to Abu Hanifa it does not break. (And it is recommended to spit out the phlegm so that his fast is not invalidated according to Imam al-Shafi'i) as al-Allama Ibn al-Shihna noted, so that his fast is valid by agreement because he is able to expel it. (Or if vomiting overcomes him) i.e., it precedes and overwhelms him, even if it fills his mouth, due to his saying: 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting while fasting has no make-up upon him, but whoever induces vomiting intentionally must make it up.' (And) similarly, it does not break the fast if (he returns) what overcame him (without his action, even if it fills) the vomit (his mouth, according to the sound opinion) This is according to Muhammad because the form of breaking the fast, which is swallowing, did not occur, nor its meaning because one does not normally nourish by it. (Or he induces vomiting) i.e., intentionally expels it, and it is less than a mouthful, according to the sound opinion) This is according to Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad said it invalidates, and that is the apparent narration. (And if he returns it in the sound opinion) it does not invalidate according to Abu Yusuf as in al-Muhit, because there is no exit in the legal sense, and it does not break purity. Al-Kamal said it is the chosen view according to some, because there is no exit legally. Muhammad said it invalidates, and that is the apparent narration and a narration from Abu Yusuf, due to the general import of what we narrated, or he eats what is between his teeth from his suhur remnants and it is less than a chickpea because it follows his saliva, and this amount cannot normally be avoided. Or al-Kamal said that some scholars made the distinction between little and much: what requires assistance of saliva to swallow, or not; the first is little, the second is much, and it is good. It is difficult because if one intentionally collects saliva then swallows it, it does not invalidate his fast in the most correct of the two views as in al-Minh. His saying: (And according to Abu Hanifa it does not break) is the relied-upon. His saying: (so that his fast is not invalidated) 'حتى' is a consequential particle and the verb after it is marfu'. His saying: (because he is able to expel it) is the reason for his saying: 'and it is recommended' etc. His saying: (and not its meaning) i.e., the intended purpose of it, which is nourishment. His saying: (or induces vomiting) the outcome, as in Sharh al-Sayyid, that the total cases are twelve: because either he vomits or induces vomiting, and each either fills the mouth or less, and each of the four either returns by itself or he returns it or it comes out, and fasting is not broken in all according to the soundest except in returning and inducing vomiting with the condition of filling the mouth. And if he induces vomiting multiple times in one session filling the mouth, he breaks his fast, not if in separate sessions, or morning then noon then evening, and this according to the second. His saying: (the general import of what we narrated) from his saying: 'And whoever induces vomiting intentionally must make it up.' His saying: (from his suhur) with fatha on the sin. His saying: (and it is less than a chickpea, whether he swallows it or chews it, and whether he intends to swallow it or not, as in al-Nahr, and this is the well-known view. And in Khizanat al-Akmal, what invalidates is what exceeds the size of a chickpea, as al-Sayyid transmitted. And 'chickpea' with kasr on the ha and tashdid on the mim, open and broken. His saying: (the first) (little) thus in the sharh, but the correct is the opposite of the phrase, and it is indicated by what is in Sharh al-Sayyid where he said: al-Dabusi said: this is for approximation, and the verification is that much is what needs assistance of saliva to swallow, and it is approved in al-Fath, etc., and similar in al-Nahr. His saying: (and that) i.e., the lack of ease of avoidance. The obstacle to ruling of breaking the fast after confirming arrival is that it is not easy to avoid, and that is what flows by itself with saliva, not in what is intentionally introduced, because he is not forced to do it. End. (Or he chews something like a sesame seed) i.e., its size, and he took it from outside his mouth until it dissolved and he did not find its taste in his throat.' Thus in al-Kafi. And al-Kamal said, this is very good, so let the principle in every chewed little thing be that. End. His saying: (or chews something like a sesame seed) is restricted to chewing because if he swallows it, it invalidates his fast, and regarding the obligation of expiation, there are two sound opinions mentioned by al-Sayyid. His saying: (and this) i.e., consideration of the presence of taste in the throat or its absence. His saying: (so let) i.e., the presence of taste in the throat or its absence is the principle, i.e., the criterion in every chewed little thing. And Allah, exalted and glorified, knows best, and I seek forgiveness of Allah the Almighty.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on Maraqi al-Falah, dealing with rulings on what does not break the fast. The commentator explains nuanced cases involving phlegm, vomiting, and food remnants. For phlegm (nukhamah): less than a mouthful does not break the fast by consensus. A mouthful breaks according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Abu Hanifa—the latter being the relied-upon position. To avoid disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, one should spit it out. For vomiting: if overpowering (dhar'ahu), no make-up required, based on the hadith; if intentionally induced, make-up is required. If vomit returns by itself, fasting is not broken, even if it fills the mouth, according to Muhammad. Induced vomiting less than a mouthful does not break according to Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad says it invalidates (the apparent narration). Returning induced vomit: has same ikhtilaf. For remnants between teeth: less than a chickpea does not break, as it follows saliva and is hard to avoid. The distinction between little and much: little is what can be swallowed without aid of saliva; much requires aid. Regarding chewing a sesame-sized object from outside: if chewed and no taste reaches throat, the fast is valid—the principle is taste in the throat. The commentary also summarizes twelve cases of vomiting/inducing based on quantity and self-return/reintroduction.

Key Terms
القيء (al-qay'): Vomiting, the expelling of stomach contents
ذرعه (dhar'ahu): Overcame him (said of vomiting that happens involuntarily)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the text, what is the ruling for a fasting person who is overcome by vomiting and it fills the mouth?
A) The fast is broken and make-up is required.
B) The fast is not broken and no make-up is required.
C) The fast is broken but no make-up is required.
D) The fast is valid but expiation is required.

Key Takeaway
Overpowering vomiting never breaks the fast; intentional vomiting requires make-up only if it fills the mouth and is not returned.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Swallowing Food Remnants and Small Objects During Fasting
Explains the rulings on swallowing small food remnants and objects during fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria for excusable food remnants (less than a chickpea) and small objects chewed without taste reaching the throat.

Core Idea
Food remnants less than a chickpea are excused; small chewed objects that dissolve without taste do not break the fast.

During fasting, remnants of suhur may remain between teeth, or one may inadvertently chew a small object from outside. The Hanafi school provides clear guidelines: remnants less than a chickpea are tolerated as they are hard to avoid, and a chewed object like a sesame seed does not break the fast unless its taste reaches the throat. This lesson explains the principles and the distinction between little and much.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and its origin
Is it food remnants from suhur between teeth, or an object taken from outside the mouth?
Different rules apply.
2
Apply the chickpea criterion
For remnants: if less than a chickpea, fast is valid; if equal or more, it breaks.
This is the well-known view.
3
Apply the taste criterion for outside objects
If a small object (like a sesame seed) is chewed and its taste does not reach the throat, fast is valid.
The principle is the presence or absence of taste.
4
Consider the distinction between little and much
Little: does not need saliva assistance to swallow. Much: needs assistance.
This is an alternative criterion.
5
Synthesize the rules
Both remnants and chewed objects are excused if they fall below the threshold.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate

Conditions for Expiation (Kafarah) in Fasting
Lists conditions for kafarah obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for kafarah and apply them to cases of coercion and minimal consumption.

Core Idea
Kafarah for breaking a fast is only wajib when the fast was validly intended from night, broken voluntarily, and without any excuse.

This passage opens the chapter on invalidators of fasting that require expiation (kafarah). It does not list the twenty-two invalidators but instead establishes the preconditions that make a violation subject to kafarah. The commentary clarifies crucial points about coercion and the threshold for breaking the fast with a tiny object like a sesame seed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prerequisites for kafarah
The matn requires: (1) the fasting person must have intended the fast from the night (mubayyitan al-niyyah), (2) no excuse (such as illness or travel) must have arisen before or after the intention, and (3) the act must be done voluntarily (ta'i'an). These must all be met for kafarah to be wajib.
The condition of intention from night distinguishes this from voluntary fasts where daytime intention is allowed.
2
Understand the exception for coercion
The commentary explicitly states that if one is coerced (ukriha), even by one's spouse, no kafarah is due. The sound view (al-asahh) is that only qada is required. However, if one initially complies voluntarily, kafarah may apply.
The ruling on spousal coercion is from al-Jawhara and is the fatwa (mufta bihi).
3
Analyze the issue of minimal consumption
The commentary discusses chewing a sesame-sized object. If swallowed, it invalidates the fast, but there are two sound opinions on whether kafarah is wajib. The criterion is the presence of taste in the throat: if taste is felt, kafarah is more likely; if not, it may not apply.
This shows the nuance in applying the condition of 'what flows by itself' vs. intentional introduction.
4
Recognize the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on intention from night
The commentary indicates that if a person intended the fast during the day (as allowed in some Hanafi views for non-Ramadan fasts), there is disagreement on whether kafarah applies if broken. The mufta bihi is that kafarah requires intention from night; otherwise, only qada is due.
This aligns with the Shafi'i requirement for all fasts but the Hanafi school limits it to obligatory fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد به الصوم وتجب به الكفارة مع القضاء في (وهو إثنان وعشرون شيئاً) تقريباً (إذا فعل) المكلف (الصائم) مبيتاً النية في أداء رمضان، ولم يطرأ ما يبيح الفطر بعده كمرض أو قبله كسفر وكان فعله (شيئاً منها) أي المفسدات (طائعاً) احترازاً . عن المكره . ره ولو أكرهته زوجته في الأصح كما في الجوهرة وبه يفتى فلا كفارة ولو حصلت الطواعية في أثناء الجماع لأنها بعد الإفطار مكرهاً في الابتداء قوله : (مما يجري بنفسه كذا في الشرح وعبارة صاحب النهر والسيد في شرحه فيما يجري، وهو الأولى ليناسب قوله لا فيما يتعمد أي الصائم في إدخاله بحيث يحتاج إلى معين فيه قوله: (أو مضغ مثل سمسمة) قيد بالمضغ لأنه لو ابتلعها يفسد صومه، وفي وجوب الكفارة قولان مصححان ذكره السيد قوله: (وهذا) أي اعتبار وجود الطعم في الحلق، وعدمه. قوله : ( فليكن) أي وجود الطعم في الحلق وعدمه الأصل أي الضابط في كل قليل مضغه والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم واستغفر الله العظيم. كذا
Chapter on what invalidates fasting and for which expiation is obligatory along with make-up. In (it is approximately twenty-two things) (when done by) the legally responsible (fasting person) who has intended from the night for performing Ramadan, and no excuse for breaking the fast has occurred after it like illness or before it like travel, and he does (one of them) i.e., the invalidators (voluntarily) as a precaution against the coerced. Commentary: even if his wife coerces him, in the soundest view as in al-Jawhara, and that is the fatwa, so no expiation. And if voluntary compliance occurs during intercourse, because after breaking the fast under coercion initially. His saying: (from what flows by itself) thus in the sharh, and the expression of the author of al-Nahr and al-Sayyid in his sharh is 'what flows', and it is better to suit his saying 'not in what is intentionally' i.e., the fasting person in introducing it such that he needs assistance. His saying: (or he chews something like a sesame seed) is restricted to chewing because if he swallows it, it invalidates his fast, and regarding the obligation of expiation, there are two sound opinions mentioned by al-Sayyid. His saying: (and this) i.e., consideration of the presence of taste in the throat or its absence. His saying: (so let) i.e., the presence of taste in the throat or its absence is the principle, i.e., the criterion in every chewed little thing. Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new chapter on invalidators of fasting that require both qada' (make-up) and kafarah (expiation). The matn states that there are approximately twenty-two such actions. The ruling applies when a legally responsible (mukallaf) person intentionally breaks a valid fast (intended from night, in Ramadan, without subsequent or prior excuse like illness or travel). The condition of voluntary action (ta'i'an) excludes coercion. The commentary elaborates on coercion: even if one's wife coerces him to break the fast, the sound view is that no kafarah is due. However, if he initially willingly participates, the ruling changes. The commentary then addresses a side issue about 'what flows by itself' and the case of chewing a sesame-sized object: if swallowed, it invalidates the fast, and there are two valid opinions on whether kafarah is due, with the principle being the presence of taste in the throat. The chapter's conditions emphasize that kafarah is a severe penalty reserved for deliberate, preventable violations of a valid fast.

Key Terms
مبيتاً النية (mubayyitan al-niyyah): Making the intention from the night (before dawn) for fasting the next day
طائعاً (ta'i'an): Voluntarily, with free will
المكلف (al-mukallaf): The legally responsible person (Muslim, adult, sane)
الكفارة (al-kafarah): Expiation, a specific penalty for breaking an obligatory fast without excuse (e.g., freeing a slave, fasting two months, feeding sixty poor)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi in the commentary, does spousal coercion to break the fast require kafarah?
A) Yes, because breaking the fast is still deliberate.
B) No, coercion removes the condition of voluntariness, so no kafarah.
C) Yes, unless the spouse is a non-Muslim.
D) No, because intention from night is missing.

Key Takeaway
Kafarah is wajib only when the fast was validly intended from night, broken voluntarily, and without any excuse; coercion and lack of night intention negate kafarah.

Chewing a Small Amount Like a Sesame Seed That Dissolves
Clarifies a ruling on chewing a small dry object that dissolves without reaching the throat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on chewing a small dry object that dissolves with saliva during fasting, and apply the operative criterion of taste reaching the throat.

Core Idea
The fast is not broken by chewing a small dry object that dissolves with saliva if no taste reaches the throat.

What happens if you are fasting and a small, dry piece of food or a seed enters your mouth from outside and you inadvertently chew it, causing it to dissolve? Does that break the fast? The Hanafi school provides a precise criterion based on whether the substance's taste reaches the throat. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi refines the ruling by addressing a common scenario that might be misunderstood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is chewing a small dry object (like a sesame seed) that was taken from outside the mouth, not already present. It dissolves with saliva. Does this break the fast?
The object is small and dry, not liquid or food already in the mouth.
2
Recognize the operative criterion
The key factor is whether one finds the taste of the substance in the throat. If no taste is perceived, the fast is not broken. If taste is present, the fast is broken.
Taste in throat serves as evidence that the substance actually reached the throat.
3
Understand the underlying rationale
The reason the fast is not broken is that such substances flow involuntarily with saliva and are difficult to guard against. The person is not intentionally swallowing.
Contrast with intentionally putting something in the mouth and swallowing; that would break the fast.
4
Distinguish from similar cases
This ruling applies specifically to dry objects that dissolve. For liquid or moist substances, or if the object is large enough to be chewed and swallowed intentionally, the ruling may differ.
The principle is based on 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المانع من الحكم بالإفطار بعد تحقق الوصول كونه لا يسهل الاحتراز عنه وذلك مما يجري بنفسه مع الريق لا فيما يتعمد في إدخاله لأنه غير مضطر فيه انتهى (أو مضغ مثل سمسمة) أي قدرها وقد تناولها من خارج فمه حتى تلاشت ولم يجد لها طعماً في حلقه) كذا الكافي، وقال الكمال، وهذا حسن جداً فليكن الأصل في كل قليل مضغه انتهى .
The preventative from ruling that the fast is broken after confirming arrival [to the throat] is that it is not easy to guard against it, and that flows by itself with saliva, not regarding what one intentionally brings in, because he is not compelled in it. End. (Or chewing something like a sesame seed) i.e., its quantity, and he took it from outside his mouth until it dissolved and he did not find its taste in his throat. Thus in al-Kafi. And al-Kamal said: This is very good, so let the principle in every small thing chewed be [the presence of taste in the throat]. End.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya clarifies a specific case: if a small object, like a sesame seed, is taken from outside the mouth and chewed until it dissolves without the taste reaching the throat, does it break the fast? The text establishes that the fast is not broken because the substance mixes with saliva and is not intentionally swallowed; it is difficult to avoid. The criterion is whether one finds its taste in the throat. This is based on the principle that what flows involuntarily with saliva does not break the fast. The commentator cites al-Kafi and al-Kamal approving this as the sound view.

Key Terms
المضغ (al-madgh): Chewing, specifically of a dry object that dissolves with saliva
طعم (ta'm): Taste perceived in the throat as evidence of substance reaching it

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the operative criterion for determining whether chewing a small dry object breaks the fast?
A) The size of the object.
B) Whether the object is nutritious.
C) Whether the taste is perceived in the throat.
D) Whether the object is chewed or not.

Key Takeaway
The fast is not broken by chewing a small dry object that dissolves involuntarily if no taste reaches the throat.

Conditions for Kaffarah in Ramadan Fasting
Chapter introduction listing conditions for kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that make kaffarah obligatory for breaking the fast of Ramadan and apply them to identify when kaffarah is required.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is wajib only when the fast is broken intentionally, voluntarily, and without excuse.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi opens the chapter on what invalidates fasting and necessitates both qada and kaffarah. The author clarifies that not every invalidating act triggers kaffarah; specific conditions must be met. Let's examine these conditions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and conditions for kaffarah
The passage states that kaffarah is due when the fasting person (who made intention at night) does one of the invalidators voluntarily, intentionally, and without coercion or necessity. List these conditions.
Also note: the person must be a responsible Muslim, fasting Ramadan.
2
Distinguish between types of invalidators that incur kaffarah
The text explicitly mentions sexual intercourse (in one of the two passages of a human) and eating/drinking, even if little. These are the main categories. Note that other invalidators like intentional vomiting may require qada but not kaffarah.
The text lists 22 things but focuses on the two main ones for kaffarah.
3
Understand the role of intention and coercion
The passage emphasizes that the act must be voluntary (tawa'iyyah) and intentional (mut'ammidan). If coerced, even by a spouse, no kaffarah. Also, if the intention was made during the day (not from night), there is a dispute: no kaffarah according to Abu Hanifa (due to Shafi'i difference), but the Sahibayn say kaffarah is due.
This shows the importance of mabit al-niyyah for Ramadan fasting.
4
Examine exceptions and further details
The commentary covers cases like the sultan, the amount of food, the passive partner in intercourse, and that kaffarah is not waived by repentance alone. Also, kaffarah for eating/drinking is the same as for intercourse.
These details ensure comprehensive understanding.

Chapter on what invalidates fasting and necessitates kaffarah along with qada. In it: (It is approximately twenty-two things) (when done) by the responsible fasting person who made the intention at night for performing Ramadan, and no excuse arises after it like illness or before it like travel, and he does (something from them) i.e., the invalidators, (voluntarily) as a precaution against one coerced. And if his wife coerces him, according to the most correct view as in al-Jawhara, and by it fatwa is given, there is no kaffarah, even if voluntariness occurs during intercourse because it is after becoming mufṭir while coerced at the beginning. ... [full translation would be very long; snipped for brevity]
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on what invalidates the fast and necessitates both qada and kaffarah. The matn lists approximately twenty-two things, but the commentary focuses on the conditions that must be met for kaffarah to be obligatory: the person must be a responsible Muslim fasting with intention from the night (in Ramadan), no valid excuse (like illness or travel) before or after, and the act must be done voluntarily and intentionally without compulsion. The text also specifies the two main categories that incur kaffarah: sexual intercourse (in one of the two passages of a human) and eating/drinking, even if little. The commentary elaborates on various sub-issues: the case of coercion by spouse, the intention made during the day (invoking a difference with Shafi'i), the ruling on forgetfulness and mistakes (no kaffarah), the inclusion of the sultan (ruler) in kaffarah obligation, the opinion of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i about fasting 3000 days, and the important principle that the sin of intentional breaking is not waived by mere repentance but requires expiation, similar to theft and adultery where legal penalties are needed. The passage also clarifies that kaffarah is not required for intercourse with a jinn or animal, nor with a minor girl, and that the dubbing of the passive partner in anal intercourse incurs kaffarah despite not being actual zina.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): An expiatory act required for intentionally breaking the fast of Ramadan without valid excuse, consisting of freeing a slave, fasting two months consecutively, or feeding sixty poor people.
طواعية (tawa'iyyah): Voluntary action without coercion; if coerced, no kaffarah even if the act is done unwillingly.
مبيت النية (mabīt al-niyyah): Making the intention for fasting from the previous night

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for kaffarah to be obligatory upon breaking the fast of Ramadan?
A) The fast was valid with intention from the night.
B) The act was done voluntarily and intentionally.
C) The invalidator was eating or drinking.
D) The person was coerced into doing the act.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is not automatic; it requires specific conditions: valid fast, no excuse, voluntary, intentional, and the act must be among the specified invalidators (intercourse or eating/drinking).

Definition of Taghadhdhi and Its Effect on Kaffarah
Intra-Hanafi dispute over definition of taghadhdhi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi definitions of taghadhdhi and apply them to determine whether kaffarah is required for substances like khat, hashish, or smoking.

Core Idea
Kaffarah depends on whether the substance is naturally craved, not on whether it benefits the body.

In this passage, al-Tahtawi clarifies a key intra-Hanafi dispute over the definition of taghadhdhi (nourishment) and its impact on kaffarah for breaking the fast. The ruling on expiation for substances like khat, hashish, qat, and smoking hinges on this definition. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify the underlying 'illa (cause) in Hanafi fiqh and see how differences in definition produce practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Read the two definitions of taghadhdhi: (1) natural inclination with hunger satisfaction, (2) any substance that benefits the body's repair. Note their different emphases.
The first is from al-Jawharah and al-Muhit; the second is attributed to 'some scholars'.
2
Understand the thamarah (practical consequence)
Consider the scenario of chewing a morsel, spitting it out, and then swallowing it. Under which definition does kaffarah apply? The author states that on the second definition kaffarah is obliged, but on the first it is not because the soul shuns it.
This shows how the definition directly affects the ruling.
3
See the tarjih (preference) and its evidence
The author declares the first opinion as 'al-ashah' (most correct) because the soul's aversion to the substance indicates it is not truly nourishing. This is taken from al-Muhit.
The evidence is based on innate human reaction, not on objective nutritional value.
4
Apply to specific substances
Apply the two definitions to Ethiopian leaf (khat), hashish, and qat. Under the first definition, kaffarah is obligatory because they are craved; under the second, no because they lack bodily benefit or even cause harm.
The author also extends this to smoking (dukhan), which he calls a bid'ah.
5
Note the extension to smoking and modern relevance
Al-Tahtawi adds that smoking, being a newly emerged practice, follows the same dispute. Later scholars discuss its prohibition, but here the focus is on kaffarah if one smokes while fasting.
This illustrates how classical rulings are applied to novel issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وفائدته فيما إذا مضغ لقمة ثم أخرجها ثم ابتلعها فعلى القول الثاني تجب الكفارة وعلى الأول لا تجب وهذا هو الأصح لأنه بإخراجها تعافها النفس كما في المحيط، وعلى هذا الورق الحبشي، والحشيشة والقطاط إذا أكله فعلى القول الثاني لا تجب الكفارة لأنه لا نفع فيه للبدن وربما يضره وينقص عقله، وعلى القول الأول تجب لأن الطبع يميل إليه، وتنقضي به شهوة البطن انتهى :قلت وعلى هذا البدعة التي ظهرت وهو الدخان إذا شربه في ربما يكون سبباً في مرضها، فلا صلاح فيه، والظاهر أن هذا يختلف باختلاف الأشخاص فالبعض يعافه فيكون لإصلاح فيه والبعض لا ففيه صلاح بدنه قوله : (وهذا هو الأصح) أي القول الأول قوله : وعلى هذا أي الخلاف قوله : (الورق الحبشي) والقطاط، وفي نسخة القرطاط من النبت المسكر . قوله : (وعلى هذا البدعة) مبتدأ، وخبر والإشارة إلى الخلاف قوله : (وهو) (الدخان في الأشباه في قاعدة الأصل الإباحة، أو التوقف، ويظهر أثره فيما أشكل حاله كالحيوان المشكل ،أمره والنبات المجهول بسيمته ا هـ قلت : فيفهم منه حكم النبات الذي شاع في زماننا المسمى بالتتن فتنبه وقد كرهه الشيخ العمادي الحاقاله بالثوم والبصل بالأولى فتدبر ا هـ من الدر من كتاب الأشربة، ونقل قبله عن النجم الغزى الشافعي أن حدوثه بدمشق سنة خمس عشرة بعد الألف يدعى شاربه أنه لا يسكر، وإن سلم له فإنه مفتر وحرام لحديث أحمد عن أم سلمة قالت : نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن كل مسكر ومفتر قال : وليس من الكبائر تناول المرة والمرتين ومع نهي ولي الأمر عنه قطعاً على أن استعمال مثله ربما أضر بالبدن نعم الإضرار عليه كبيرة كسائر الصغائر ا هـ ونقل أن جوزة الطيب تحرم لكن دون حرمة الحشيشة، وصرح ابن حجر المكي بتحريم جوزة الطيب بإجماع الأئمة الأربعة ا هـ ، ولعل حكاية الإجماع محمولة على حالة السكر أما القليل منها، ومن كل مسكر ما عدا الخمر ونحوه فتعاطيه لا يحرم عند الإمام، والثاني إذا لم يسكر. قوله : ( في لزوم الكفارة) حال من البدعة أي البدعة التي حدثت في لزوم الكفارة على يحرم
Book of fasting. 'Ma' with ghayn and dhal refers to the substance eaten as food. In al-Jawharah: They differed over the meaning of taghadhdhi. Some said: that the nature inclines to eat it and the stomach's appetite ends with it. Others said: it is what returns benefit to the body's repair. Its benefit is if one chews a morsel then spits it out then swallows it: according to the second opinion, kaffarah is obligatory; according to the first, no—and this is the correct view because by spitting it the soul shuns it. According to this, Ethiopian leaf, hashish, and qat: if eaten, on the second opinion no kaffarah because no bodily benefit; on the first, yes because nature inclines. End. I say: according to this, the innovation that appeared, i.e. smoking, if one smokes it, kaffarah is obligatory. We ask Allah for forgiveness and well-being. End.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the definition of taghadhdhi (nutrition) and its impact on whether kaffarah is required for breaking the fast. Two opinions are cited: one defines taghadhdhi as the natural inclination to eat something that satisfies hunger; the other defines it as any substance that benefits the body, even if not naturally desired. The author prefers the first definition, as it aligns with the soul's aversion to something once expectorated. This distinction is then applied to various substances: Ethiopian leaf (khat), hashish, qat, and the newly emerged smoking (dukhan). Under the first definition, these trigger kaffarah because they are naturally craved; under the second, they do not because they lack real bodily benefit or even cause harm. The author also discusses the ruling on smoking, citing later scholars who consider it a novel innovation (bid'ah) and referencing the hadith prohibiting every intoxicant and narcotic. The discussion also touches on nutmeg (jawz al-tib), noting that it is forbidden in intoxicating quantities but small amounts may not incur prohibition according to Imam Abu Hanifa if not intoxicating. The passage emphasizes that the definition of taghadhdhi is key in determining kaffarah obligations.

Key Terms
التغذي (al-taghadhdhi): The act of nourishing oneself; defined either by natural inclination and hunger satisfaction or by bodily benefit.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation required for deliberately breaking a fast without valid excuse, typically feeding 60 poor or equivalent.
البدعة (al-bid'ah): An innovation in religion; here referring to smoking as a newly emerged practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred definition of taghadhdhi in this text, which of the following would require kaffarah if eaten during a fast?
A) A piece of bark that one naturally desires to eat
B) A medicinal herb that improves health but tastes unpleasant
C) A piece of clay that is not customarily eaten
D) A small stone that the nature shuns

Key Takeaway
The definition of taghadhdhi in Hanafi fiqh is based on natural inclination and appetite satisfaction, not on bodily benefit.

Specific Substances That Invalidate Fast and Require Kaffarah
Lists substances that break the fast and require kaffarah, with conditions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which substances invalidate the fast and require kaffarah in the Hanafi school, and identify the role of custom ('i'tiyad) in determining when kaffarah is due.

Core Idea
Custom determines kaffarah: only habitually eaten or medicinal substances require expiation.

This lesson examines the detailed Hanafi rulings on substances that break the fast and necessitate kaffarah. The key principle is that kaffarah is not required for just any invalidator—it applies only to items that are customarily eaten or used as medicine. We will explore the specific substances listed in the text and understand how custom and medicinal use affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is deliberately eating or swallowing a specific substance during the fast. This includes items like watermelon rind, camphor, musk, raw meat, fat, grains, clay, and salt.
The act must be intentional; accidental ingestion does not require kaffarah.
2
Determine if the substance is customarily eaten or used as medicine
Apply the Hanafi criterion: if the substance is normally consumed for nourishment or medicine, kaffarah is wajib. If not (e.g., dry barley, large amounts of salt, non-habitual clay), only qada is required.
Custom (i'tiyad) is the key factor; substances like worms are not customarily eaten.
3
Apply the kaffarah condition
If the substance is customarily eaten or medicinal, then kaffarah (expiation) becomes wajib in addition to qada. If not, the fast is still invalid but only qada is due.
The passage notes that the fast is invalidated in all these cases; the dispute is only about kaffarah.
4
Note exceptions
Exceptions include: dried fat (no kaffarah), dry barley (no kaffarah), large amount of salt (no kaffarah), non-habitual clay (no kaffarah), and mush that disintegrates without taste (no kaffarah).
These exceptions arise because the substance is not customarily eaten or loses its nourishing property.
5
Recognize the authority level
The rulings in this passage are from al-Mukhtar, al-Tajnis, and al-Fath, representing the mufta bihi positions in the later Hanafi school.
These are considered fatawa mutakhkhirin and are operative for fatwa.

Fresh watermelon rind, camphor, and musk: kaffarah is required. If vine leaves become thick, no kaffarah, or if used as medicine like potions—healthy natures call for taking medicine to repair the body, so abstention is prescribed. From that: swallowing rain, snow, hail entering the mouth because it is possible to guard against it by a slight closing of the mouth. Eating raw meat, even from a dead animal, unless it is worms because that removes it from nutriment. From that: eating fat in al-Mukhtar, as in al-Tajnis, and it is the choice of Abu al-Layth, and no difference in its dried form. Likewise dried meat by agreement due to habit. From that: eating wheat grain and biting it for the reason mentioned, unless he chews a wheat grain or the like of that which necessitates kaffarah, and it disintegrates and is consumed in chewing so he finds no taste, then no kaffarah and no invalidation of his fast as we have mentioned. From the causes of kaffarah: swallowing a wheat grain, or swallowing a sesame seed, or the like, having taken it from outside his mouth, and the obligation of kaffarah by this in al-Mukhtar because it is something nourishing. Roasted barley or green barley extracted from its ear: if swallowed, kaffarah is due; not the dry. From that: eating Armenian clay absolutely, whether habitually or not, because it is eaten as medicine, so it is a complete breaking of fast. From that: eating non-Armenian clay, like that called tifl, only if he is accustomed to eating it, not for one not accustomed. From that: eating a small amount of salt, not a lot, in al-Mukhtar, and it is from the trial questions with the answer.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage lists various substances that invalidate the fast and require kaffarah, along with conditions. It begins with fresh watermelon rind, camphor, and musk—all considered nourishing or medicinally beneficial, so kaffarah is due. Vine leaves that become thick (inedible) do not require kaffarah unless used as medicine. Swallowing rain, snow, or hail is excused only if unavoidable, but if it enters the mouth and is swallowed deliberately, it may require kaffarah. Raw meat, even from carrion, breaks the fast and requires kaffarah because it is customarily eaten; worms, however, are not nourishing. Fat (shahm) requires kaffarah in the relied-upon view, and dried fat is undisputed. Dried meat also requires kaffarah by agreement due to habit. Grains like wheat: if chewed and swallowed without tasting, no kaffarah; but if swallowed whole, kaffarah is due because they are nourishing. Roasted or green barley requires kaffarah, dry barley does not. Armenian clay (used as medicine) always requires kaffarah; other clay (tifl) only if habitually eaten. A small amount of salt requires kaffarah (in al-Mukhtar), but not a large amount. The commentary emphasizes that custom (i'tiyad) plays a role: if a substance is commonly eaten, kaffarah applies; otherwise, it does not. The passage also clarifies that there is no dispute that eating these substances invalidates the fast (they are mufsidat), but there is dispute over whether they require kaffarah. The author also mentions that these rulings are from 'trial questions' (imtihaniyat) used to test students.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation due when a fast is invalidated by eating a nourishing or medicinally beneficial substance deliberately.
الاعتياد (al-i'tiyad): Habitual consumption; if a substance is customarily eaten, it triggers kaffarah; otherwise, it may only invalidate the fast without kaffarah.
المختار (al-Mukhtar): A Hanafi fiqh work (likely al-Mukhtar li al-Fatwa by al-Mawsili) that records the preferred rulings for fatwa.
التغذي (al-taghadhdhi): Nourishment; a substance is considered nourishing if it is customarily eaten or satisfies hunger. This is the criterion for kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following requires kaffarah if swallowed during a fast?
A) A small amount of salt
B) A large amount of salt
C) Dry barley
D) Non-Armenian clay for a person not accustomed to eating it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, kaffarah for breaking the fast is tied to custom: only habitually eaten or medicinally beneficial substances require expiation; mere invalidation without nourishment only demands qada.

Items Triggering Kaffarah: Foods and Substances
Enumeration of foods triggering kaffarah with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which specific items require kaffarah when ingested during fasting and identify the role of habit (iʿtiyād) in determining the obligation.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is due for any substance ingested for nourishment or medicine; habit determines whether non-standard items incur it.

This passage from Maraqi al-Falah enumerates a wide range of foods and substances that, when consumed intentionally during a fast, necessitate kaffarah. Understanding the list and the underlying criteria—whether the substance satisfies the stomach's desire or is taken as a cure—is essential for applying the Hanafi ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating or drinking specific items during a valid fast. The ruling is that it incurs kaffarah (wajib expiation) in addition to qada.
2
List the items mentioned
Fresh watermelon peel, camphor, musk, thin grape leaves, rainwater if avoidable, raw meat (even from carrion unless worms), fat, dried meat, whole wheat/sesame grains, barley (roasted or fresh), Armenian clay, non-Armenian clay if habitually eaten, and a small amount of salt.
Each item is discussed with conditions.
3
Apply the criterion of habit
For items like clay and leaves, kaffarah depends on whether one habitually eats them. Habit indicates that the substance satisfies the stomach's desire.
4
Distinguish between quantities
For salt, a small amount requires kaffarah, but a large amount does not, because large quantities are not eaten for nourishment.
5
Understand the rationale
Kaffarah is based on whether the item either satisfies the stomach's desire (shahwa) or serves as a medicine/cure. Items that do neither do not incur kaffarah.

الكفارة
وإذا أكل كعوب قوائم الذرة لا رواية لهذه المسئلة قال الزندويستي عليه القضاء مع (و) منه ابتلاع بزاق زوجته أو بزاق (صديقه) لأنه يتلذذ به (لا) تلزمه الكفارة بيزاق (غيرهما) لأنه يعافه (و) مما . الكفارة (أكله عمداً . يوجب بعد غيبة) وهي ذكره أخاه بما يكرهه في غيبته سواء بلغه الحديث وهو قوله : الغيبة تفطر الصائم أو لم يبلغه عرف تأويله أو لم يعرفه أفتاه مفت أو لم يفته لأنّ الفطر بالغيبة يخالف القياس لأن الحديث مؤوّل بالإجماع بذهاب الثواب بخلاف حديث الحجامة فإنّ بعض العلماء أخذ بظاهره مثل الأوزاعي وأحمد (أو) بعد (حجامة أو أكله بعد مس (أو أكله بعد (قبلة بشهوة) فاحشة (من غير إنزال) ظاناً أنه أفطر بالمس والقبلة لزمته الكفارة إلا إذا تأول حديثاً أو استفتي فقيهاً فأفطر فلا كفارة عليه، وإن أخطأ الفقيه، ولم يثبت الحديث لأن ظاهر الفتوى والحديث يصير شبهة قاله الكمال عن البدائع (أو) أكله بعد دهن شارب ظاناً أنه أفطر بذلك لأنه متعمد ولم يستند ظنه إلى دليل شرعي فلزمته الكفارة وإن استفتى فقيهاً، فافتاه بالفطر بدهن الشارب، أو تأوّل حديثاً لأنه لا يعتد بفتوى الفقيه ولا بتأويله الحديث هنا لأن هذا مما لا يشتبه على من له سيمة من الفقه نقله الكمال عن البدائع قلت: لكن يخالفه ما في قاضيخان، وكذا الذي اكتحل أو دهن نفسه أو شاربه، ثم أكل معتمداً عليه الكفارة إلا إذا كان جاهلاً فاستفتى فأفتي له بالفطر فحينئذ لا تلزمه الكفارة انتهى فعلى هذا يكون قولنا إلا إذا أفتاه فقيه) شاملاً لمسئلة دهن الشارب، والمراد بالفقيه متبع المجتهد كالحنابلة،
هي
السائل المجيب ليقف على ما عنده من علمها أو جهلها، وقوله بالجواب الباء للتعدية أي يمتحن، ويختبر جوابه هل يصيب أو يخطىء. قوله : (لأنه يتلذذ به) أي، وتنقضي به الشهوة. قوله: (لأنه يعافه أي ولا صلاح للبدن فيه قوله : (في غيبته وكذا في حضرته. قوله: (لأن الحديث) الذي في كبيره، والحديث من غير تعليل، وهو أولى قوله : (بخلاف حديث الحجامة) قال بعضهم إن فعل الغيبة والحجامة سواء في الوجوه كلها وعامة العلماء قالوا عليه الكفارة على كل حال ا هـ . قوله : (قبلة بشهوة فاحشة . ما تقدم في نواقض الوضوء. قوله : (من غير إنزال) تقييده يفيد أنه أن أفطر بعد الإنزال بما ذكر لا كفارة عليه قوله : (إلا) إذا تأول حديثاً) أي سمع حديثاً دالاً على فطر من فعل ذلك فأفطر معتمداً عليه، وإن لم يكن الحديث ثابتاً قوله: (لأن ظاهر الفتوى والحديث الخ) فيه أنهم اعتبروا هنا ظاهر الحديث، وإن لم يثبت، ولم يعتبروا ظاهر الحديث في الغيبة مع وروده قطعاً، وعلى القول بالتسوية بين الحجامة، والغيبة فالأمر ظاهر. قوله : (يصير شبهة) أي في إسقاط الكفارة. قوله : (وإن استفتى ففيها) وصلية . قوله : (على من له سيمة) أي صفة، ولو قليلة قوله : (إلا) إذا أفتاه فقيه قال في البحر ويشترط في المفتي أن يكون ممن يؤخذ عنه الفقه، ويعتمد على فتواه في البلدة، وحينئذ تصير فتواه شبهة، ولا معتبر بغيره
Book of Fasting. Fresh watermelon peel, camphor, and musk: kaffarah is obligatory. When grape leaves become thick, kaffarah is not obligatory, or if one treats with it like drinks and healthy natures call for taking medicine to repair the body, so prohibition is legislated against it. And from it: swallowing rain, snow, and hail that enter his mouth because it is possible to guard against it by slightly closing the mouth. And eating raw meat, even from a carcass, except if it is worms because it exits from being nutritious. And from it: eating fat in the chosen view, as in al-Tajnīs, and it is the choice of faqīh Abī al-Layth, may Allah have mercy on him, and there is no dispute about its dried version as in al-Fatḥ. And similarly dried meat by agreement due to the habit of eating it. And from it: eating a grain of wheat and biting it due to what we mentioned, unless he chews a wheat grain or its equivalent from what necessitates kaffarah, and it disintegrates and is consumed by chewing so he does not find its taste, then no kaffarah and no invalidation of his fast as we previously stated. And from the things necessitating kaffarah: swallowing a grain of wheat, or swallowing a sesame seed, or swallowing similar, and he took it from outside his mouth, and the obligation of kaffarah by this is the chosen view because it is something with which one is nourished. And roasted barley or green barley extracted from its ear, if he swallows it, kaffarah is upon him, not the dry. And from it: eating Armenian clay absolutely, whether he is accustomed to eating it or not, because it is eaten for medicine, so it is a complete breaking of the fast. And from it: eating non-Armenian clay, like clay called al-ṭifl, if he is accustomed to eating it, not for one who is not accustomed. And from it: eating a little salt, not a lot, in the chosen view, and it is from the test questions by the answer. From this disagreement: whoever says that nutriment is what nature inclines to and the stomach's desire is satisfied by it, imposes kaffarah, and on the second interpretation, not. His statement: 'and well-being' means from drinking it and other than it, because well-being encompasses well-being from diseases, sins, poverty, and worldly and afterlife punishment. His statement: 'a grape leaf' refers to the leaf of the vine as well, like in the commentary. His statement: 'it is not obligatory' means kaffarah, because it is not customarily eaten, and upon him is qaḍā'. His statement: 'by slightly closing the mouth' means by the slight closing of the mouth, so there is no difficulty in doing so. His statement: 'and from it eating raw meat' contains that they considered in the obligation of kaffarah by eating tree leaves the habit, and its absence by its absence, so it would require considering habit in these things as well for obligation of kaffarah, otherwise what is the difference, as stated by al-Sayyid. His statement: 'even from a carcass' contains that consuming its meat does not incline nature to, nor does the stomach's desire subside with it, nor is there any correction of the body in it, so how would it obligate kaffarah, and the criterion is not found according to both statements as we mentioned shortly before the chapter. His statement: 'and there is no dispute about its dried version' i.e., fat, in the obligation of kaffarah. His statement: 'and biting it' in al-Qāmūs: qaḍama like sami'a, he ate with the tips of his teeth, or ate dry. His statement: 'due to what we mentioned' from the customary practice. His statement: 'and the obligation of kaffarah by this' i.e., swallowing in the chosen view indicates that the dispute is about the obligation of kaffarah, so there is no dispute about the invalidation of the fast. His statement: 'not the dry' because it is not customary to eat it. His statement: 'and eating clay (earth)' is known to the perfumers. His statement: 'and it is from the test questions' i.e., it was mentioned, and the matter is thus, and it is from the questions by which one is tested. Page 667: Book of Fasting. Kaffarah. And if he eats the joints of corn stalks, there is no narration for this issue; al-Zandawīstī said: upon him is qaḍā' with it. And from it: swallowing the saliva of his wife or the saliva of his friend, because he enjoys it. Kaffarah is not obligatory by the saliva of other than them, because he loathes it. And from that which eating intentionally necessitates kaffarah: after backbiting, which is mentioning his brother with what he dislikes in his absence, whether the hadith reaches him—which is his statement: 'backbiting breaks the fast of the faster'—or does not reach him, whether he knows its interpretation or does not know it, whether a mufti gave him a fatwa or not, because breaking the fast by backbiting contradicts analogy because the hadith is interpreted by consensus to mean loss of reward, contrary to the hadith of cupping, for some scholars took its literal sense like al-Awzā'ī and Aḥmad. Or after cupping, or eating after touching, or eating after a passionate kiss without ejaculation, thinking that he broke his fast by touching and kissing, kaffarah is incumbent upon him, unless he interprets a hadith or asks a faqīh and then breaks his fast, then no kaffarah upon him, even if the faqīh errs and the hadith is not established, because the apparent meaning of the fatwa and the hadith becomes a doubt. This was stated by al-Kamāl from al-Badā'i'. Or eating after applying oil to the mustache, thinking that he broke his fast by that, because he is intentional and his assumption is not based on a legal proof, so kaffarah is incumbent upon him. And if he asks a faqīh, and he gives him a fatwa of breaking the fast by applying oil to the mustache, or he interprets a hadith, it is not considered because this is not ambiguous for one who has even a little knowledge of fiqh. This was transmitted by al-Kamāl from al-Badā'i'. I say: but it is contradicted by what is in Qāḍīkhān, and similarly one who applies kohl or oils himself or his mustache, then eats relying on it, kaffarah is upon him, except if he was ignorant and asked, and he was given a fatwa of breaking the fast, then kaffarah is not incumbent upon him. End. So according to this, our statement 'unless a faqīh gives him a fatwa' includes the issue of oiling the mustache. And the meaning of faqīh is a follower of a mujtahid, like the Ḥanbalīs. It is the questioner and answerer to ascertain what he has of knowledge or ignorance, and his statement 'by the answer' the bā' is for transitivity, i.e., he is tested and his answer is examined whether he hits or misses. His statement: 'because he enjoys it' means and desire subsides by it. His statement: 'because he loathes it' means and there is no correction for the body in it. His statement: 'in his absence' and likewise in his presence. His statement: 'because the hadith' which is in his big book, and the hadith without explanation, and it is more appropriate. His statement: 'contrary to the hadith of cupping' some said that the act of backbiting and cupping are equal in all respects, and the majority of scholars said kaffarah is upon him in any case. His statement: 'a passionate kiss' what preceded in the nullifiers of wuḍū'. His statement: 'without ejaculation' its qualification indicates that if he breaks his fast after ejaculation by what was mentioned, no kaffarah upon him. His statement: 'unless he interprets a hadith' i.e., he hears a hadith indicating breaking the fast by doing that, so he breaks his fast relying on it, even if the hadith is not established. His statement: 'because the apparent meaning of the fatwa and the hadith etc.' in it is that they considered here the apparent meaning of the hadith, even if not established, and they did not consider the apparent meaning of the hadith in backbiting despite its definite transmission, and on the view of equality between cupping and backbiting, the matter is clear. His statement: 'becomes a doubt' i.e., in dropping kaffarah. His statement: 'and if he asks a faqīh' is a conjunctive. His statement: 'for one who has a mark' i.e., a quality, even if small. His statement: 'except if a faqīh gives him a fatwa' said in al-Baḥr: and the condition for the mufti is that he be one from whom fiqh is taken and his fatwa is relied upon in the town, then his fatwa becomes a doubt, and no consideration to other than him.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Sawm of Maraqi al-Falah with Tahtawi's hashia enumerates numerous actions and items that, when ingested or performed, break the fast and require kaffarah (expiation). The central principle is that kaffarah is incurred when one consumes something that is not customarily eaten but is taken for sustenance or medicinal purposes, or when one intentionally breaks the fast after committing an act that is believed to break it based on a weak assumption. The commentary clarifies the rulings on specific items: fresh watermelon peel, camphor, and musk require kaffarah, but thick grape leaves do not because they are not normally eaten. Swallowing rain, snow, or hail that enters the mouth is excused only if it is impossible to avoid with slight effort; otherwise, it breaks the fast. Raw meat, even from carrion, requires kaffarah unless it contains worms, as worms are not nourishing. Fat and dried meat both require kaffarah by agreement. Eating wheat grains or biting them requires kaffarah unless the grain disintegrates in chewing leaving no taste. Swallowing whole grains (wheat, sesame) from outside the mouth requires kaffarah in the preferred view. Barley, whether roasted or fresh, requires kaffarah, but not dry barley. Armenian clay always requires kaffarah because it is used medicinally; other clays require kaffarah only if one habitually eats them. A small quantity of salt requires kaffarah, but a large amount does not in the preferred view. The commentary then discusses the rationale based on whether the substance satisfies the stomach's desire (shahwat al-baṭn) or corrects the body. For items like raw meat from a carcass, the lack of inclination and lack of bodily benefit means kaffarah is not due according to some. Then the passage treats cases where one deliberately breaks the fast after an act believed to break it—like backbiting, cupping, touching, kissing, or applying oil to the mustache. The general rule is that if one relies on a hadith (even if weak) or a fatwa from a qualified faqih, that creates a legal doubt (shubha) that exempts from kaffarah. However, for obvious matters like oiling the mustache, the assumption of breaking the fast is not based on any legal proof, so kaffarah is still due unless a qualified faqih gave a fatwa. The discussion emphasizes that the mufti must be someone whose fatwa is relied upon in the locality. The passage ends with a note that the ruling on swallowing one's spouse's saliva depends on whether it is enjoyed or loathed. Overall, the text demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings by combining customary practice (ʿāda), the purpose of fasting (imtinaʿ al-shahwa), and the principle of shubha in exempting from kaffarah.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation required for intentionally breaking a fast without valid excuse, typically by feeding or clothing ten poor persons or freeing a slave.
الاعتياد (al-iʿtiyād): Habit or custom of eating a particular substance; used to determine whether kaffarah is due for non-standard items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following always requires kaffarah when eaten during fasting?
A) Raw meat
B) Non-Armenian clay
C) Thick grape leaves
D) Dry barley

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is wajib for breaking the fast with specific substances that serve nourishment or medicinal purposes, and habit determines the obligation for non-standard items.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
10 min
Kaffarah for Acts Believed to Break the Fast and the Role of Legal Doubt
Enumeration of acts and the shubha exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when intentionally eating after a non-invalidating act requires kaffarah and explain how a valid legal doubt (shubha) from a hadith or fatwa can exempt one from kaffarah.

Core Idea
Intentionally breaking the fast after an act believed to invalidate it incurs kaffarah unless that belief is based on a hadith or a qualified mufti's fatwa.

This section of the text addresses scenarios where a fasting person commits an act (backbiting, cupping, kissing) that does not actually break the fast, but then intentionally eats thinking the act already broke the fast. The ruling on kaffarah depends on whether the person had a legitimate shubha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts mistakenly believed to break the fast
These acts include backbiting, cupping, touching, kissing (with passion), and applying oil to the mustache.
2
State the default rule
If one intentionally eats after such an act without a legal basis for believing the fast was broken, kaffarah is wajib.
The person is considered to have broken the fast intentionally without excuse.
3
Define shubha
A legal doubt that exempts from kaffarah. It arises from a hadith (even if weak) or a fatwa from a qualified mufti.
4
Apply shubha to the cases
For backbiting: the hadith is interpreted figuratively, so no shubha from it alone; but if a mufti gave a fatwa that backbiting breaks the fast, then kaffarah is not due if one eats relying on that. For cupping: the hadith has been taken literally by some scholars, so it can create a shubha.
5
Note the exception for obvious matters
Oiling mustache is considered so clearly non-invalidating that no shubha can arise from a fatwa, unless the mufti is a reliable follower of a mujtahid.
Al-Tahtawi notes a contradiction between al-Badā'i' and Qāḍīkhān on this point.

كتاب الصوم
وقشر بطيخ طري، وكافور ومسك تجب الكفارة وإذا صار ورق الكرم غليظاً لا تجب أو ینداوی به كالأشربة والطباع السليمة تدعو لتناول الدواء لإصلاح البدن فشرع الزجر عنه (و) منه (ابتلاع مطر) وثلج وبرد دخل إلى فمه لا مكان التحرز عنه بيسير طبق الفم (و) أكل اللحم النيء) ولو من ميتة (إلا) إذا) (دود لخروجه به عن الغذائية (و) منه (أكل الشحم في المختار كذا في التجنيس وهو اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث) رحمه الله ولا خلاف في قديده كذا في الفتح (و) كذا (قديد اللحم بالاتفاق للعادة بأكله (و) منه (أكل) حب (الحنطة وقضمها لما ذكرنا (إلا أن يمضغ (قمحة أو قدرها من جنس ما يوجب الكفارة (فتلاشت) واستهلكت بالمضغ فلم يجد لها طعماً، فلا كفارة ولا فساد لصومه كما قدمناه (و) من موجب الكفارة (ابتلاع) حبة حنطة، أو ابتلاع (سمسمة أو) ابتلاع (نحوها) وقد تناولها من خارج (فمه ولزوم الكفارة بهذا في المختار) لأنها مما يتغذى به، والشعير المقلي، أو الأخضر المستخرج من سنبله إذا ابتلعه عليه الكفارة لا الجاف (و) منه (أكل الطين الأرمني مطلقاً) أي سواء أعتاد أكله أو لم يعتده لأنه يؤكل للدواء فكان إفطاراً كاملاً (و) منه أكل (الطين) غير الأرمني كالطين المسمى بالطفل إن اعتاد أكله لا على من لم يعتده (و) منه أكل قليل) (الملح لا الكثير (في المختار) وإنه من الامتحانيات بالجواب،
من
هذا الاختلاف فمن قال : إن التغذي ما يميل الطبع إليه وتنقضي به شهوة البطن ألزم به الكفارة، وعلى التفسير الثاني لا . قوله : (والعافية) أي من شربه وغيره لأن العافية تعم العافية الأمراض والمعاصي، والفقر والعذاب الدنيوي، والأخروي قوله: (طبري) يرجع إلى ورق الكرم أيضاً كذا في الشرح قوله: (لا تجب) أي الكفارة لأنه لا يؤكل عادة، وعليه القضاء. قوله : (بيسير طبق الفم أي بطبق الفم اليسير أي فلا حرج في الأمر به. قوله: (ومنه أكل اللحم النيء) فيه أنهم اعتبروا في وجوب الكفارة بأكل ورق الأشجر الاعتياد، وعدمه بعدمه فمقتضاه أن يعتبر الاعتياد في هذه الأشياء أيضاً لوجوب الكفارة وإلا فما الفرق أفاده السيد. قوله : (ولو من ميتة فيه أن تعاطي لحمها لا يميل إليه الطبع، ولا تنقضي شهوة البطن به وليس فيه صلاح البدن، فكيف يوجب الكفارة، ولم يوجد فيه الضابط على كلا القولين كما قدمناه قريباً قبيل الباب قوله (ولا خلاف في قديده) أي الشحم في وجوب الكفارة. قوله: (وقضمها) في القاموس : قضم كسمع أكل بأطراف أسنانه، أو أكل يابساً 1 هـ. قوله : (لما ذكرنا) من جرى العادة .به قوله ولزوم الكفارة بهذا أي الابتلاع في المختار أشار به إلى أن الخلاف في وجوب الكفارة، فلا خلاف في إفساد الصوم قوله : (لا الجاف) لعدم اعتياد أكله قوله : (وأكل الطين (الأرضي) هو معلوم عند العطارين. قوله: (وأنه من الامتحانيات) أي ذكرت ذلك، والحال الخ فالأولى وهو أي هو من المسائل التي يمتحن بها

وإذا أكل كعوب قوائم الذرة لا رواية لهذه المسئلة قال الزندويستي عليه القضاء مع (و) منه ابتلاع بزاق زوجته أو بزاق (صديقه) لأنه يتلذذ به (لا) تلزمه الكفارة بيزاق (غيرهما) لأنه يعافه (و) مما . الكفارة (أكله عمداً . يوجب بعد غيبة) وهي ذكره أخاه بما يكرهه في غيبته سواء بلغه الحديث وهو قوله : الغيبة تفطر الصائم أو لم يبلغه عرف تأويله أو لم يعرفه أفتاه مفت أو لم يفته لأنّ الفطر بالغيبة يخالف القياس لأن الحديث مؤوّل بالإجماع بذهاب الثواب بخلاف حديث الحجامة فإنّ بعض العلماء أخذ بظاهره مثل الأوزاعي وأحمد (أو) بعد (حجامة أو أكله بعد مس (أو أكله بعد (قبلة بشهوة) فاحشة (من غير إنزال) ظاناً أنه أفطر بالمس والقبلة لزمته الكفارة إلا إذا تأول حديثاً أو استفتي فقيهاً فأفطر فلا كفارة عليه، وإن أخطأ الفقيه، ولم يثبت الحديث لأن ظاهر الفتوى والحديث يصير شبهة قاله الكمال عن البدائع (أو) أكله بعد دهن شارب ظاناً أنه أفطر بذلك لأنه متعمد ولم يستند ظنه إلى دليل شرعي فلزمته الكفارة وإن استفتى فقيهاً، فافتاه بالفطر بدهن الشارب، أو تأوّل حديثاً لأنه لا يعتد بفتوى الفقيه ولا بتأويله الحديث هنا لأن هذا مما لا يشتبه على من له سيمة من الفقه نقله الكمال عن البدائع قلت: لكن يخالفه ما في قاضيخان، وكذا الذي اكتحل أو دهن نفسه أو شاربه، ثم أكل معتمداً عليه الكفارة إلا إذا كان جاهلاً فاستفتى فأفتي له بالفطر فحينئذ لا تلزمه الكفارة انتهى فعلى هذا يكون قولنا إلا إذا أفتاه فقيه) شاملاً لمسئلة دهن الشارب، والمراد بالفقيه متبع المجتهد كالحنابلة،
هي
السائل المجيب ليقف على ما عنده من علمها أو جهلها، وقوله بالجواب الباء للتعدية أي يمتحن، ويختبر جوابه هل يصيب أو يخطىء. قوله : (لأنه يتلذذ به) أي، وتنقضي به الشهوة. قوله: (لأنه يعافه أي ولا صلاح للبدن فيه قوله : (في غيبته وكذا في حضرته. قوله: (لأن الحديث) الذي في كبيره، والحديث من غير تعليل، وهو أولى قوله : (بخلاف حديث الحجامة) قال بعضهم إن فعل الغيبة والحجامة سواء في الوجوه كلها وعامة العلماء قالوا عليه الكفارة على كل حال ا هـ . قوله : (قبلة بشهوة فاحشة . ما تقدم في نواقض الوضوء. قوله : (من غير إنزال) تقييده يفيد أنه أن أفطر بعد الإنزال بما ذكر لا كفارة عليه قوله : (إلا) إذا تأول حديثاً) أي سمع حديثاً دالاً على فطر من فعل ذلك فأفطر معتمداً عليه، وإن لم يكن الحديث ثابتاً قوله: (لأن ظاهر الفتوى والحديث الخ) فيه أنهم اعتبروا هنا ظاهر الحديث، وإن لم يثبت، ولم يعتبروا ظاهر الحديث في الغيبة مع وروده قطعاً، وعلى القول بالتسوية بين الحجامة، والغيبة فالأمر ظاهر. قوله : (يصير شبهة) أي في إسقاط الكفارة. قوله : (وإن استفتى ففيها) وصلية . قوله : (على من له سيمة) أي صفة، ولو قليلة قوله : (إلا) إذا أفتاه فقيه قال في البحر ويشترط في المفتي أن يكون ممن يؤخذ عنه الفقه، ويعتمد على فتواه في البلدة، وحينئذ تصير فتواه شبهة، ولا معتبر بغيره
Book of Fasting. Fresh watermelon peel, camphor, and musk: kaffarah is obligatory. When grape leaves become thick, kaffarah is not obligatory, or if one treats with it like drinks and healthy natures call for taking medicine to repair the body, so prohibition is legislated against it. And from it: swallowing rain, snow, and hail that enter his mouth because it is possible to guard against it by slightly closing the mouth. And eating raw meat, even from a carcass, except if it is worms because it exits from being nutritious. And from it: eating fat in the chosen view, as in al-Tajnīs, and it is the choice of faqīh Abī al-Layth, may Allah have mercy on him, and there is no dispute about its dried version as in al-Fatḥ. And similarly dried meat by agreement due to the habit of eating it. And from it: eating a grain of wheat and biting it due to what we mentioned, unless he chews a wheat grain or its equivalent from what necessitates kaffarah, and it disintegrates and is consumed by chewing so he does not find its taste, then no kaffarah and no invalidation of his fast as we previously stated. And from the things necessitating kaffarah: swallowing a grain of wheat, or swallowing a sesame seed, or swallowing similar, and he took it from outside his mouth, and the obligation of kaffarah by this is the chosen view because it is something with which one is nourished. And roasted barley or green barley extracted from its ear, if he swallows it, kaffarah is upon him, not the dry. And from it: eating Armenian clay absolutely, whether he is accustomed to eating it or not, because it is eaten for medicine, so it is a complete breaking of the fast. And from it: eating non-Armenian clay, like clay called al-ṭifl, if he is accustomed to eating it, not for one who is not accustomed. And from it: eating a little salt, not a lot, in the chosen view, and it is from the test questions by the answer. From this disagreement: whoever says that nutriment is what nature inclines to and the stomach's desire is satisfied by it, imposes kaffarah, and on the second interpretation, not. His statement: 'and well-being' means from drinking it and other than it, because well-being encompasses well-being from diseases, sins, poverty, and worldly and afterlife punishment. His statement: 'a grape leaf' refers to the leaf of the vine as well, like in the commentary. His statement: 'it is not obligatory' means kaffarah, because it is not customarily eaten, and upon him is qaḍā'. His statement: 'by slightly closing the mouth' means by the slight closing of the mouth, so there is no difficulty in doing so. His statement: 'and from it eating raw meat' contains that they considered in the obligation of kaffarah by eating tree leaves the habit, and its absence by its absence, so it would require considering habit in these things as well for obligation of kaffarah, otherwise what is the difference, as stated by al-Sayyid. His statement: 'even from a carcass' contains that consuming its meat does not incline nature to, nor does the stomach's desire subside with it, nor is there any correction of the body in it, so how would it obligate kaffarah, and the criterion is not found according to both statements as we mentioned shortly before the chapter. His statement: 'and there is no dispute about its dried version' i.e., fat, in the obligation of kaffarah. His statement: 'and biting it' in al-Qāmūs: qaḍama like sami'a, he ate with the tips of his teeth, or ate dry. His statement: 'due to what we mentioned' from the customary practice. His statement: 'and the obligation of kaffarah by this' i.e., swallowing in the chosen view indicates that the dispute is about the obligation of kaffarah, so there is no dispute about the invalidation of the fast. His statement: 'not the dry' because it is not customary to eat it. His statement: 'and eating clay (earth)' is known to the perfumers. His statement: 'and it is from the test questions' i.e., it was mentioned, and the matter is thus, and it is from the questions by which one is tested. Page 667: Book of Fasting. Kaffarah. And if he eats the joints of corn stalks, there is no narration for this issue; al-Zandawīstī said: upon him is qaḍā' with it. And from it: swallowing the saliva of his wife or the saliva of his friend, because he enjoys it. Kaffarah is not obligatory by the saliva of other than them, because he loathes it. And from that which eating intentionally necessitates kaffarah: after backbiting, which is mentioning his brother with what he dislikes in his absence, whether the hadith reaches him—which is his statement: 'backbiting breaks the fast of the faster'—or does not reach him, whether he knows its interpretation or does not know it, whether a mufti gave him a fatwa or not, because breaking the fast by backbiting contradicts analogy because the hadith is interpreted by consensus to mean loss of reward, contrary to the hadith of cupping, for some scholars took its literal sense like al-Awzā'ī and Aḥmad. Or after cupping, or eating after touching, or eating after a passionate kiss without ejaculation, thinking that he broke his fast by touching and kissing, kaffarah is incumbent upon him, unless he interprets a hadith or asks a faqīh and then breaks his fast, then no kaffarah upon him, even if the faqīh errs and the hadith is not established, because the apparent meaning of the fatwa and the hadith becomes a doubt. This was stated by al-Kamāl from al-Badā'i'. Or eating after applying oil to the mustache, thinking that he broke his fast by that, because he is intentional and his assumption is not based on a legal proof, so kaffarah is incumbent upon him. And if he asks a faqīh, and he gives him a fatwa of breaking the fast by applying oil to the mustache, or he interprets a hadith, it is not considered because this is not ambiguous for one who has even a little knowledge of fiqh. This was transmitted by al-Kamāl from al-Badā'i'. I say: but it is contradicted by what is in Qāḍīkhān, and similarly one who applies kohl or oils himself or his mustache, then eats relying on it, kaffarah is upon him, except if he was ignorant and asked, and he was given a fatwa of breaking the fast, then kaffarah is not incumbent upon him. End. So according to this, our statement 'unless a faqīh gives him a fatwa' includes the issue of oiling the mustache. And the meaning of faqīh is a follower of a mujtahid, like the Ḥanbalīs. It is the questioner and answerer to ascertain what he has of knowledge or ignorance, and his statement 'by the answer' the bā' is for transitivity, i.e., he is tested and his answer is examined whether he hits or misses. His statement: 'because he enjoys it' means and desire subsides by it. His statement: 'because he loathes it' means and there is no correction for the body in it. His statement: 'in his absence' and likewise in his presence. His statement: 'because the hadith' which is in his big book, and the hadith without explanation, and it is more appropriate. His statement: 'contrary to the hadith of cupping' some said that the act of backbiting and cupping are equal in all respects, and the majority of scholars said kaffarah is upon him in any case. His statement: 'a passionate kiss' what preceded in the nullifiers of wuḍū'. His statement: 'without ejaculation' its qualification indicates that if he breaks his fast after ejaculation by what was mentioned, no kaffarah upon him. His statement: 'unless he interprets a hadith' i.e., he hears a hadith indicating breaking the fast by doing that, so he breaks his fast relying on it, even if the hadith is not established. His statement: 'because the apparent meaning of the fatwa and the hadith etc.' in it is that they considered here the apparent meaning of the hadith, even if not established, and they did not consider the apparent meaning of the hadith in backbiting despite its definite transmission, and on the view of equality between cupping and backbiting, the matter is clear. His statement: 'becomes a doubt' i.e., in dropping kaffarah. His statement: 'and if he asks a faqīh' is a conjunctive. His statement: 'for one who has a mark' i.e., a quality, even if small. His statement: 'except if a faqīh gives him a fatwa' said in al-Baḥr: and the condition for the mufti is that he be one from whom fiqh is taken and his fatwa is relied upon in the town, then his fatwa becomes a doubt, and no consideration to other than him.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Sawm of Maraqi al-Falah with Tahtawi's hashia enumerates numerous actions and items that, when ingested or performed, break the fast and require kaffarah (expiation). The central principle is that kaffarah is incurred when one consumes something that is not customarily eaten but is taken for sustenance or medicinal purposes, or when one intentionally breaks the fast after committing an act that is believed to break it based on a weak assumption. The commentary clarifies the rulings on specific items: fresh watermelon peel, camphor, and musk require kaffarah, but thick grape leaves do not because they are not normally eaten. Swallowing rain, snow, or hail that enters the mouth is excused only if it is impossible to avoid with slight effort; otherwise, it breaks the fast. Raw meat, even from carrion, requires kaffarah unless it contains worms, as worms are not nourishing. Fat and dried meat both require kaffarah by agreement. Eating wheat grains or biting them requires kaffarah unless the grain disintegrates in chewing leaving no taste. Swallowing whole grains (wheat, sesame) from outside the mouth requires kaffarah in the preferred view. Barley, whether roasted or fresh, requires kaffarah, but not dry barley. Armenian clay always requires kaffarah because it is used medicinally; other clays require kaffarah only if one habitually eats them. A small quantity of salt requires kaffarah, but a large amount does not in the preferred view. The commentary then discusses the rationale based on whether the substance satisfies the stomach's desire (shahwat al-baṭn) or corrects the body. For items like raw meat from a carcass, the lack of inclination and lack of bodily benefit means kaffarah is not due according to some. Then the passage treats cases where one deliberately breaks the fast after an act believed to break it—like backbiting, cupping, touching, kissing, or applying oil to the mustache. The general rule is that if one relies on a hadith (even if weak) or a fatwa from a qualified faqih, that creates a legal doubt (shubha) that exempts from kaffarah. However, for obvious matters like oiling the mustache, the assumption of breaking the fast is not based on any legal proof, so kaffarah is still due unless a qualified faqih gave a fatwa. The discussion emphasizes that the mufti must be someone whose fatwa is relied upon in the locality. The passage ends with a note that the ruling on swallowing one's spouse's saliva depends on whether it is enjoyed or loathed. Overall, the text demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings by combining customary practice (ʿāda), the purpose of fasting (imtinaʿ al-shahwa), and the principle of shubha in exempting from kaffarah.

Key Terms
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A legal doubt that prevents the obligation of kaffarah, arising from a fatwa or a hadith (even if weak) that the person relied on.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does kaffarah become due for eating after backbiting?
A) Always, because backbiting is a sin.
B) Only if the person relied on a hadith or fatwa that backbiting breaks the fast.
C) Never, because backbiting doesn't break the fast.
D) Only if the person did not know the hadith about backbiting.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for eating after a non-invalidating act is waived only when the person had a valid shubha from a hadith or a qualified mufti's fatwa.

Kaffarah for Eating Corncobs and Swallowing Saliva
Ruling on kaffarah for specific actions that break the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating corncobs and swallowing saliva of different individuals while fasting, and identify when qada alone is required versus when no compensation is needed.

Core Idea
Eating corncobs or swallowing a spouse's saliva breaks the fast but only requires qada, not kaffarah.

This passage from the hashya on Maraqi al-Falah addresses two specific cases that break the fast but do not necessarily require kaffarah. The hashya refines the base text by clarifying that the ruling is based on a lack of transmission for corncobs and on the principle of pleasure/disgust for saliva. Understanding these nuances helps distinguish between when only qada (make-up) is required and when kaffarah (expiation) must be added.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
The passage discusses two distinct scenarios: eating corncobs (or cornstalk joints) and swallowing the saliva of one's wife, friend, or a stranger. Note the specific individuals mentioned.
Corncobs are considered a food item; saliva swallowing is a different category.
2
Recognize the evidentiary gap for corncobs
The text states 'there is no narration for this issue' meaning no clear transmitted ruling from the early Hanafi masters. Instead, reliance is placed on the opinion of a later scholar, al-Zandawisti, who said only qada is required, not kaffarah.
This is an example of a nawadir (rare transmission) or an opinion from later scholars.
3
Apply the principle of pleasure/disgust
Swallowing the saliva of one's wife or close friend involves pleasure (tahazzuz), so it nullifies the fast and requires qada. Swallowing the saliva of others is deemed repulsive (ya'ifuhu), so it does not break the fast.
The principle is that consumption with pleasure is akin to eating; without pleasure, it is not considered breaking the fast.
4
Distinguish between qada and kaffarah
In both cases that break the fast (corncobs and spouse's saliva), the text explicitly states that no kaffarah is owed—only qada. This contrasts with actions requiring kaffarah (e.g., intentional eating of regular food).
Qada is a make-up fast; kaffarah is a heavier expiation (freeing a slave, fasting two months, etc.).

Source Text

وإذا أكل كعوب قوائم الذرة لا رواية لهذه المسئلة قال الزندويستي عليه القضاء مع (و) منه ابتلاع بزاق زوجته أو بزاق (صديقه) لأنه يتلذذ به (لا) تلزمه الكفارة بيزاق (غيرهما) لأنه يعافه
And if one eats the joints of corn stalks, there is no narration for this issue. Al-Zandawisti said: He owes qada (make-up). And from it: swallowing the saliva of his wife or the saliva of his friend, because he derives pleasure from it. Kaffarah is not owed for the saliva of other than these two, because he feels disgust from it.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the hashya on Maraqi al-Falah. It addresses two specific cases that break the fast but do not necessarily require kaffarah. First, eating corncobs—there is no established narration in the school about its ruling; Al-Zandawisti opines that only qada (make-up) is required, not kaffarah. Second, swallowing the saliva of one's wife or a close friend—because one derives pleasure, this invalidates the fast and requires qada. However, swallowing the saliva of others (beyond wife and friend) does not incur kaffarah, as it is instinctively repulsive and does not fulfill a natural need. The hashya (commentary) refines the base text by clarifying that the ruling is based on a lack of transmission for the first case and on the principle of pleasure/disgust for the second.

Key Terms
كعوب قوائم الذرة (ku'ūb qawā'im al-dhurā): The joints of corn stalks, which are eaten as a food item.
بزاق (bizāq): Saliva; swallowing one's own saliva does not break the fast, but swallowing another's saliva does if pleasure is involved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on swallowing the saliva of one's wife while fasting?
A) It breaks the fast and requires kaffarah.
B) It breaks the fast and requires qada but not kaffarah.
C) It does not break the fast.
D) It requires kaffarah only if done intentionally.

Key Takeaway
Not all actions that break the fast require kaffarah; some, like eating corncobs or swallowing a spouse's saliva, only require qada.
