Disliked Acts for the Fasting Person
Enumeration of three disliked acts with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the disliked acts explicitly mentioned in the text and identify the conditions under which they become permissible or invalidate the fast.

Core Idea
Seven acts are makruh tahrimi; necessity can make some permissible, but substance entry invalidates.

Fasting is not just about avoiding food and drink; certain actions risk breaking the fast and are therefore disliked. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi lists these actions, explains why they are disliked, and provides important exceptions for genuine need. Understanding these nuances helps a fasting person avoid invalidating their fast while navigating daily necessities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disliked acts
Read the text to find the three explicitly stated disliked acts: tasting something, chewing without excuse, and chewing gum (al-‘ilk). The text says seven are disliked but focuses on these.
The other four are not expanded in this passage; the lesson covers what is present.
2
Understand the ruling level
The text uses 'kareh' (disliked) which the commentator explains means karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike). This is close to haram and must be avoided unless there is a valid excuse.
Not all disliked acts are equal; some are tanzihi (lightly disliked), but here it is tahrimi.
3
Examine exceptions and conditions
For each disliked act, note the conditions under which it becomes permissible: tasting for an ill-tempered husband, chewing for a child if no alternative, and gum that does not reach the stomach.
Necessity (darura) or genuine need can override the dislike.
4
Distinguish between disliked and invalidating
Some acts are only disliked but do not break the fast; others, if substance enters the stomach, invalidate it. For example, chewing black gum that dissolves invalidates; white gum intact does not.
The risk of invalidation is why these are disliked.
5
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani restricted the dislike of tasting to obligatory fasts only, but the majority includes supererogatory fasts. The mufta bihi is the majority view, as the text sides with 'the school'.
The ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school; the relied-upon position is the broader application.

Chapter on what is disliked for the fasting person and what is not disliked and what is permitted. Seven things are disliked for the fasting person: tasting something, because it exposes the fast to invalidation, even in a supererogatory fast according to the school; and chewing without an excuse, like a woman who finds someone to chew food for her child because she is menstruating; but if she finds no alternative, there is no harm in her chewing to protect the child. There is disagreement if one fears being cheated in buying food by tasting; and for a woman to taste food if her husband is ill-tempered to know its saltiness; but if he is good-tempered, it is not permissible for her; similarly for the female slave; I say: similarly for the hired servant; and disliked is chewing gum (al-‘ilk) which is mastic gum, or said to be frankincense, from which nothing reaches the stomach with saliva. Chapter: On what is disliked for the fasting person. The apparent unrestricted use of dislike indicates that intended is prohibitive dislike (karahah tahrimiyyah). His saying: 'tasting something' similarly his saliva, or a woman fasting supererogatorily without her husband's permission unless he is sick or fasting or ... and his farting in water, being in ihram for Hajj or Umrah, and he cannot prevent his wife in these situations. And the male and female slave cannot fast supererogatorily without the master's permission, and he can prevent them even if they are sick, fasting, or in ihram. And the husband can make his wife break the fast, and the master can make the slave break. The woman makes it up if her husband gives permission or she is divorced; the slave makes it up if the master gives permission or he is freed. And the hired servant should not fast supererogatorily without the employer's permission if his fasting harms the service; if it does not harm, he may do so without permission. As for a man's daughter, mother, and sister, they may fast supererogatorily without his permission. The apparent unrestricted use of dislike indicates prohibition. His saying: 'because it exposes the fast to invalidation' because the pulling force is strong and it cannot be trusted that nothing is drawn inward. His saying: 'even in a supererogatory fast according to the school' and whoever restricted it to obligatory fasts, like Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and the negation of dislike of tasting in supererogatory fasts is only according to the ruling permitting breaking a supererogatory fast without excuse, as in the commentary. His saying: 'who chews' with fatha on the ضاد. His saying: 'and there is disagreement if one fears being cheated' some disapproved it, and some scholars said in obligatory fasting: it is disliked for him to taste something if he has an alternative; if he has no alternative, because he needs to buy food and fears that if he does not taste it, he will be cheated or it will not suit him, then it is not disliked, so in supererogatory fasting it is even more so. His saying: 'ill-tempered' i.e., regarding that matter; hence the commentary said: ill-tempered meaning he harasses her about the saltiness of food, too little salt; but if he is ill-tempered in other matters, it is not permitted for her. His saying: 'it is not permissible for her' indicates that the dislike is prohibitive, as previously. His saying: 'similarly the hired servant' i.e., for cooking. His saying: 'from which nothing reaches' but if something reaches from it, such as if it is black absolutely, whether chewed or not, because black dissolves with chewing, or if it is white and not chewed, or chewed but not enclosed, then it invalidates the fast. And what is smelled of the smell of urine due to chewing frankincense? That is from the smell not from the body, for foul smell changes the color of silver, and if a rose is placed in water it changes its scent, but nothing separates from its essence.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage lists seven disliked actions for a fasting person, with emphasis that the karahah is tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike, close to haram). The first is tasting anything, because it risks drawing substance into the throat, invalidating the fast even in a supererogatory fast according to the relied-upon school position. Chewing food without excuse is also disliked, e.g., a woman chewing food for her child while she is fasting and has no one else to do it; if unavoidable, it is permissible. There is disagreement on tasting when buying food: if one fears being cheated, some scholars permit it in obligatory fasts, and more so in supererogatory. For a woman, tasting her cooking to check saltiness is only allowed if her husband is ill-tempered and likely to scold her; otherwise it is not allowed. Similarly, a female slave or hired cook may taste if needed. Chewing gum (al-‘ilk, i.e., mastic gum or frankincense) is disliked, but only from which no part separates and enters the stomach; black gum that dissolves with chewing invalidates the fast, as does white gum if chewed without enclosing it in a wrapper. The text also discusses related rulings: women fasting supererogatorily without husband's permission, slaves without master's permission, and hired servants without employer's permission if fasting harms their work. The overall message is to avoid actions that risk breaking the fast, but concessions exist for genuine need.

Key Terms
الكراهة التحريمية (al-karahah al-tahrimiyyah): Prohibitive dislike; an action that is not haram but close to it, avoided out of caution.
العلك (al-'ilk): Chewing gum; here refers specifically to mastic gum (mustaka) or frankincense (luban) that does not disintegrate into the stomach.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate

Seven Disliked Acts for the Fasting Person
Lists and explains seven disliked acts for the fasting person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven disliked acts for a fasting person according to the Hanafi school, explain why each is disliked, and identify the exceptions that make an act permissible.

Core Idea
Avoid acts that risk the fast or create suspicion; many have valid exceptions.

Fasting is more than avoiding food and drink; it also requires refraining from acts that could invalidate or compromise its reward. The Hanafi school classifies certain acts as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because they either risk breaking the fast or place the fasting person in a position of suspicion. In this lesson, we examine the seven disliked acts detailed in Hashiya al-Tahtawi, along with their specific exceptions and the evidence behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling Level
Note that the passage uses the term 'karahah' (disliked) for all seven acts. The commentator clarifies that this is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), meaning it is sinful to do so without a valid excuse.
The ruling is based on the risk of corrupting the fast or creating suspicion, not on the act being inherently haram.
2
List the Seven Acts
The seven acts are: (1) tasting food or drink, (2) chewing something that might reach the stomach, (3) chewing gum (mastic/frankincense), (4) kissing, (5) obscene direct contact, (6) gathering saliva and swallowing it, and (7) doing anything that weakens the body and risks breaking the fast.
Some acts have more than one expression in the text, but these are the core ones.
3
Analyze the Reasons for Dislike
For each act, identify the illa (effective cause): tasting risks drawing substance into the stomach; chewing gum risks dissolution and also creates suspicion; kissing and direct contact risk arousal leading to invalidation; gathering saliva mimics drinking; weakening acts may cause the fast to be broken.
The hadith 'Whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day should not stand in places of accusation' is cited to support the dislike of acts that create suspicion.
4
Identify Exceptions and Conditions
Exceptions include: tasting when buying food to avoid being cheated; a woman tasting for an ill-tempered husband; chewing gum if nothing reaches the stomach; kissing and direct contact if one is completely secure from arousal (but the relied-upon view is still disliked for obscene contact).
The text details these exceptions, showing that the ruling is not absolute.
5
Apply the Evidence
The proof for the dislike is a combination of the risk of corruption (istidlal bi al-ma'na) and the hadith about avoiding accusation. The commentator uses the hadith to support the ruling for chewing gum and similar acts.
The evidence is zanni in both transmission and meaning, hence the ruling is at the level of karahah tahrimi, not haram.

والعذر وهو كتسهيل ريح، وتقليل بخر بفمه. قوله: (وقيل يباح لهم قال فخر الإسلام قال : ولكن يستحب للرجال تركه . قوله : (وكره له القبلة الخ) التفصيل في غير القبلة الفاحشة أما هي، وهي يمص شفتها فيكره على الإطلاق، والجماع فيما دون الفرج كالقبلة في ظاهر الرواية هندية، والمراد بالجماع المباشرة، والمعانقة يجري فيها التفصيل على المشهور نهر قوله : ( والمباشرة الفاحشة) هي أن يتعانقا وهما متجردان، ويمس فرجه فرجها وظاهره أنها على هذا التفصيل وفي الهندية الصحيح أن المباشرة الفاحشة تكره، وإن أمن بل نقل عن المحيط عدم الخلاف في كراهتها. قوله: (الإنزال، أو الجماع) فلا بد من الأمن منهما حتى تنتفي الكراهة فإن خشي أحدهما ثبتت الكراهة قاله السيد في الحاشية قوله لما فيه أي فيما ذكر من القبلة، والمباشرة قوله: (بعاقبة الفعل) متعلق بالفساد. قوله : (بمضغ شفتها) متعلق بالفاحش، والباء للسببية، والأولى بمص، والمراد به الأخذ بأطراف الأسنان تحاشياً عن الشبهة أي شبهة المفطر كالماء قوله : لما فيه من تعريض الإفساد عبارة الشرح لما فيه من تعريضه للإفساد، والضمير للصوم، وهو من إضافة المصدر إلى مفعوله قوله : (للدليل) أي لأجل ذكر الدليل عليها .
Fasting chapter, section on what is disliked for the fasting person and what is not disliked and what is recommended for him. (Seven things are disliked for the fasting person: tasting something because it risks corrupting the fast, even if it's a voluntary fast according to the school.) And chewing it without excuse is disliked, like a woman who finds someone to chew food for her child as if she is breaking her fast due to menstruation, but if she has no alternative, there is no harm in her chewing to protect the child. There is disagreement regarding when one fears being cheated in buying food to taste it. And for a woman to taste food if her husband is ill-tempered to know its saltiness, and if he is good-tempered, it is not permissible for her. Similarly for the slave woman, and similarly for the hired servant. (And) chewing gum is disliked, something that goes into the stomach with saliva. Gum is mastic, and it is said it is frankincense that has nothing that reaches from it. Section: on what is disliked for the fasting person. The apparent general application of 'disliked' indicates that it is meant as prohibited (tahrimi). He said: (tasting something) similarly his spittle, or a woman voluntarily without her husband's permission unless he is sick, or fasting, or in ihram, and he does not have the right to prevent the wife in these cases. And the slave and female slave may not fast voluntarily except with the master's permission, and he may prevent them, even if sick, fasting, or in ihram. And the husband may cause his wife to break her fast, and the master may cause his slave or female slave to break their fast. And the woman makes up the fast if her husband gives her permission or she is divorced, and the slave makes up if the master gives permission or is emancipated. And a hired worker should not fast voluntarily except with the employer's permission if his fasting harms his work, but if it does not harm, he may do so without permission. As for a man's daughter, mother, and sister, they may fast voluntarily without his permission. And the apparent general application of 'disliked' indicates prohibition. He said: because it risks corrupting the fast, because the suction is strong so it is not safe that something may be drawn from it into the interior carefully. He said: (even if on the school) and whoever restricts it to obligatory fasting like Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and denies the dislikedness of tasting in voluntary fast only on the narration of permissibility of breaking a voluntary fast without excuse, as in the Sharh. He said: (who chews) with fatha on the dad. He said: (and there is disagreement regarding when one fears being cheated) some disliked it, and some scholars said in obligatory fasting: tasting is only disliked if he has an alternative; if he has no alternative, such as needing to buy food and fearing that if he does not taste it he may be cheated or it will not suit him, it is not disliked. So voluntary fast is similar by greater reason. He said: (ill-tempered) meaning regarding that matter, hence in the Sharh: ill-tempered means he harasses her about the saltiness of food and its lack of salt; if he is ill-tempered in other matters, it is not permissible for her. He said: so it is not permissible for her, indicating that the dislikedness is prohibitive, and it has passed. He said: similarly the hired servant, i.e., for cooking. He said: (from which nothing reaches) but if something does reach from it, such as if it is completely black, whether chewed or not, because black dissolves with chewing, or if it is white and not chewed or is chewed and he is not covering his mouth, then it invalidates the fast. And the smell of urine emanating from chewing frankincense is from the smell, not from the substance, because a bad smell changes the color of silver, and rose placed in water changes its smell without any part of its essence separating. He said: (because he is accused of breaking the fast) is the reason for dislikedness, meaning it is not permissible to stand in places of accusation. He said: 'Whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day should not stand in places of accusation.' He said: (I warn you) meaning I caution you against doing it. He said: (Even if you have an excuse) meaning the excuse for it. He said: (It is recommended for women) because it takes the place of the siwak for them due to the weakness of their teeth, as they may not tolerate the siwak, so it is feared for the gums and teeth from it as in al-Fath. And it appears that it takes the place of siwak even if used not in the state of wudu, and it appears that they do not obtain the promised reward for siwak except with intention, as is the case with siwak. He said: (And disliked for men) and what appears in al-Fath is that it is a prohibitive dislikedness, and his expression: 'The first is dislikedness for men except for need, because the evidence, namely the resemblance to women, necessitates it in their case in the absence of a counterargument.' He said: (except in seclusion) added in al-Durr: with an excuse, so the dislikedness is not removed except by two conditions: seclusion and excuse, such as easing wind and reducing bad breath. He said: (And it is said it is permissible for them) said Fakhr al-Islam: but it is recommended for men to leave it. He said: (And kissing is disliked for him) the detail applies to other than obscene kissing; as for it, which is sucking her lip, it is disliked absolutely, and intercourse below the private part is like kissing in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwaya). And the meaning of intercourse is direct contact, and embracing is subject to the detail according to what is well-known. He said: (and obscene direct contact) is that they embrace while both are naked, and his private part touches her private part. And it appears that it is on this detail, and in al-Hindiya the correct view is that obscene direct contact is disliked even if he is secure; indeed it is transmitted from al-Muhit that there is no disagreement on its dislikedness. He said: (ejaculation or intercourse) so one must be secure from both for dislikedness to be negated; if he fears either, the dislikedness is established, as said by al-Sayyid in the Hashiya. He said: (because of the consequence of the act) is related to invalidating the fast. He said: (by chewing her lip) is related to 'obscene', and the ba is for causation, and the first is by sucking, meaning taking with the edges of the teeth to avoid suspicion, i.e., the suspicion of a fast-breaker like water. He said: (because it risks corruption) is the expression of the Sharh: because it risks corruption of the fast, and the pronoun is for the fast, and it is an instance of attributing the masdar to its object. He said: (for the evidence) i.e., for the purpose of mentioning the evidence for it.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage from Al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah discusses rulings on disliked acts for a fasting person. The commentator begins by explaining that the text's general use of 'disliked' indicates a prohibitive dislikedness (karahah tahrimiyyah), meaning it is sinful to perform these acts. The first disliked act is tasting food because it risks drawing substance into the stomach and breaking the fast. Even for a voluntary fast, it is disliked according to the relied-upon position. There are exceptions: a woman may taste food if her husband is ill-tempered to check seasoning, and a person may taste food when buying it to avoid being cheated. The second disliked act is chewing gum (especially mastic or frankincense) because it may dissolve and enter the stomach, and also because it creates suspicion of breaking the fast. The gum is only problematic if something actually reaches the stomach; if it is the type that does not dissolve, it is not disliked. The third set of disliked acts includes kissing and direct contact (mubasharah) even if one feels safe from ejaculation or intercourse in the apparent narration, because it risks leading to something that invalidates the fast. However, there is a distinction: if one fears ejaculation or intercourse, it is undoubtedly disliked; if secure, some say it is not disliked based on the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss while fasting. The commentator notes that the correct view is that obscene direct contact is disliked absolutely. The fourth disliked act is intentionally gathering saliva in the mouth and then swallowing it, to avoid suspicion of having swallowed water. The fifth is doing anything that one believes will weaken him for fasting, such as cupping, bloodletting, or strenuous work, because it risks causing him to break the fast. The commentator then discusses details about chewing gum: for women it is recommended as a substitute for siwak due to weak teeth, but for men it is disliked unless needed for a valid excuse in seclusion. Overall, the principle is to avoid anything that risks the validity of the fast or places one in a position of suspicion.

Key Terms
ذوق (dhawq): Tasting food to check flavor while fasting
علك (‘ilk): Chewing gum, specifically mastic or frankincense that does not dissolve
قبلة (qubla): Kissing one's spouse while fasting
مباشرة فاحشة (mubāsharah fāḥishah): Obscene direct contact such as embracing naked with genital contact
جمع الريق (jam‘ al-rīq): Gathering saliva in the mouth and deliberately swallowing it
ما ظن أنه يضعفه (mā ẓanna annahu yuḍ‘ifuhu): Any act one believes will weaken one for fasting, like cupping or hard labor

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is chewing gum disliked for a fasting person according to this passage?
A) Because it is an innovation
B) Because it may dissolve and enter the stomach, and it creates suspicion of breaking the fast
C) Because it causes bad breath
D) Because it is forbidden entirely

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines seven specific disliked acts for the fasting person, each with exceptions based on need, to preserve the integrity of the fast and avoid suspicion.

Sawm — Fasting

Nine Acts Not Disliked for the Fasting Person
Lists permissible acts with conditions and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine acts that are not disliked for the fasting person and state the condition for each, citing the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Kissing and touching are allowed while fasting, provided you are secure from arousal.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya clarifies common misconceptions about what breaks the fast. While many assume all intimate contact is forbidden, the Hanafi school permits kissing and touching with security, supported by the hadith of Aisha. The passage also lists other permissible acts such as using a wet siwak, rinsing the mouth, bathing, and even eating forgetfully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall ruling
Determine that the nine acts are ruled as 'not disliked' (la yukrah), meaning they are permissible without sin, but may become disliked if conditions are violated.
The ruling applies to each act individually, subject to its own condition.
2
Understand the condition for kissing and touching
Note that kissing (qubla) and direct contact (mubasharah) are permitted only 'with security' (ma'a al-amn) from ejaculation or intercourse. If one fears arousal, they become disliked.
Security is subjective; the fasting person must assess his own state.
3
Recognize the evidence
The author cites the hadith narrated by Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss while fasting. This is the primary dalil for permissibility.
The hadith is in both Sahih Bukhari and Muslim, making it highly authentic in transmission.
4
Apply the pattern to other acts
For each of the remaining seven acts, identify the specific condition that prevents dislikedness: no exaggeration in rinsing, not weakening in the bathhouse, kohl not reaching the throat, forgetfulness for eating/drinking, etc.
These conditions are derived from general fiqh principles or specific proofs mentioned elsewhere.
5
Distinguish from disliked or invalidating acts
Recognize that violating the condition (e.g., swallowing water while rinsing, kissing with arousal) changes the ruling to disliked or even invalidating the fast.
The passage focuses on what is not disliked; opposite cases are not fully detailed here.

(And nine things are not disliked for the fasting person) which, even if known by understanding, it is permissible to mention them for the evidence: kissing and direct contact with security from ejaculation and intercourse, based on what is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss while fasting. He said: (And nine things are not disliked for the fasting person) i.e., as he indicated by his saying: (which, even if known by understanding, it is permissible to mention them for the evidence) i.e., for the purpose of mentioning the evidence for them. Then he mentioned the evidence by his saying: (kissing and direct contact with security) etc. He said: (based on what is narrated from Aisha: that the Prophet used to kiss while fasting) the hadith. He said: (that) i.e., that he used to kiss, and the complete hadith is in the two Sahihs. He said: (with security) i.e., from ejaculation and intercourse, so if he fears either, it is disliked. He said: (and the evidence) i.e., for the absence of dislikedness with security. He said: (and it has preceded) i.e., in the discussion of kissing and direct contact. He said: (and kissing) i.e., of the wife. He said: (and direct contact) i.e., with the hand. He said: (and siwak) i.e., the wet one. He said: (and rinsing the mouth and nose) i.e., without exaggeration. He said: (and bathing) i.e., with cold water. He said: (and entering the bathhouse) i.e., if he does not fear weakness. He said: (and henna) i.e., dye. He said: (and kohl) i.e., only that which does not reach the throat. He said: (that he eats or drinks forgetfully) i.e., it is not disliked. He said: (or pours water on his head) i.e., to cool down. He said: (or wets his garment) i.e., to cool. He said: (and pushing away sleep from his eyes) i.e., by closing them or putting something on them.
Source pages p. 680

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on acts for a fasting person, listing nine things that are not disliked. The commentator explains that the author mentions them even though they are obvious to provide the supporting evidence. The first two are kissing and direct contact (with the hand) provided one is secure from ejaculation or intercourse, based on the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss his wife while fasting. If one fears arousal, these become disliked. Other acts include: using a wet siwak (toothstick), rinsing the mouth and nose without exaggeration, bathing with cold water, entering a bathhouse if it does not weaken him, applying henna, applying kohl that does not reach the throat, eating or drinking forgetfully (which does not break the fast nor is disliked), pouring water on the head or wetting the garment to cool down, and pushing away sleep from the eyes. The common principle is that these acts either have a proof of permissibility or do not risk breaking the fast. The commentator uses the hadith from Aisha as the primary evidence for kissing and direct contact.

Key Terms
قبلة (qubla): Kissing one's spouse while fasting
مباشرة باليد (mubāsharah bil-yad): Touching with the hand, not genital contact
سواك رطب (siwāk raṭb): Fresh, moist toothstick
مضمضة واستنشاق بغير مبالغة (maḍmaḍah wa istinshāq bi-ghayr mubālaghah): Rinsing mouth and nose without excessive force
اغتسال بماء بارد (ightisāl bi-mā’ bārid): Bathing with cold water to cool down
أكل أو شرب ناسياً (akl aw shurb nāsiyan): Eating or drinking forgetfully while fasting

Key Takeaway
The nine acts are not disliked if their conditions are met, with the hadith of Aisha as evidence for kissing and touching.

Sawm — Fasting

Actions Disliked and Permitted for the Fasting Person
Rulings on makruh and non-makruh acts during fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh and non-makruh acts for a fasting person, apply the condition of security from ejaculation to kissing and touching, and identify the dalil and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Kissing and touching during fasting: not inherently disliked; ruling depends on security from ejaculation.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi provides a detailed analysis of acts that are disliked (makruh) and not disliked for a fasting person. It covers the rulings on chewing bitter almond, kissing, direct touching, collecting saliva, and actions that weaken the fast. A key point is the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding kissing and touching when one feels secure from ejaculation. The relied-upon position, based on the hadith of Aisha, is that these acts are not disliked when security is present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their default ruling
List all actions mentioned: chewing bitter almond, kissing, touching, collecting saliva, oiling mustache, kohl, cupping, bloodletting, siwak. Note which are listed as makruh and which as not makruh.
Bitter almond is makruh because of suspicion of breaking the fast; siwak is sunnah at any time.
2
Analyze the condition for kissing and touching
The ruling depends on 'security from ejaculation/intercourse'. If secure, it is not disliked in zahir al-riwaya. If not secure, it is makruh.
This distinction is based on the hadith of Aisha and the principle of avoiding fitna.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (in a riwaya from Hasan ibn Ziyad) holds that kissing/touching is always makruh, even with security, because it does not lack temptation. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad in the six books) disagree.
The mufta bihi is the majority view: not disliked with security.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
الكندر لأنه يتهم بالإفطار بمضغه سواء المرأة والرجل قال الإمام علي رضي الله عنه : إياك وما يسبق إلى العقول إنكاره وإن كان عندك اعتذاره وفي غير الصوم يستحب للنساء وكره للرجال إلا في خلوة وقيل : يباح لهم (و) كره له (القبلة والمباشرة) الفاحشة وغيرها (إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسه الإنزال أو الجماع في ظاهر الرواية لما فيه من تعريض الصوم على الفساد بعاقبة الفعل ويكره التقبيل الفاحش بموضع شفتها كما في الظهيرية (و) كره له (جمع الريق في الفم) قصداً ثم ابتلاعه) تحاشياً عن الشبهة (و) كره له فعل (ما ظن أنه يضعفه) عن الصوم (كالفصد والحجامة والعمل الشاق لما فيه من تعريض الإفساد (وتسعة أشياء لا تكره للصائم) وهي، وإن علمت بالفمهوم ساغ ذكرها للدليل القبلة والمباشرة مع الأمن) من الإنزال والوقاع لما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها : أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام كان يقبل ويباشر ، وهو صائم رواه الشيخان وهذا ظاهر الرواية وعن محمد أنه كره وهي رواية الحسن عن الإمام لأنها لا تخلو عن فتنة، وفي الجوهرة، وقيل إنَّ المباشرة تكره، وإن أمن على الصحيح، وهي أن يمس فرجه فجرها ودهن الشارب يفتح الدال على أنه مصدر وبضمها على إقامة اسم العين مقام المصدر لأنه ليس فيه شيء ينافي الصوم (والكحل) لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام اكتحل وهو صائم ( والحجامة) التي لا تضعفه عن الصوم (والقصد كالحجامة وذكر شيخ الإسلام أنّ شرط الكراهة ضعف يحتاج فيه إلى العطر (و) لا يكره له السواك آخر النهار بل هو سنة كأوّله لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: من
Book of Fasting: Bitter almond because he is accused of breaking the fast by chewing it, same for woman and man. Imam Ali (RA) said: Beware of what the intellect rejects even if you have an excuse for it. Outside of fasting, it is recommended for women and disliked for men except in seclusion; and it is said: permitted for them. And disliked for him is kissing and direct touching, immodest or otherwise, if he does not feel safe from ejaculation or intercourse in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) because it exposes the fast to corruption by the consequence of the act. And immodest kissing by sucking her lip is disliked as in al-Zahiriya. And disliked for him is intentionally collecting saliva in the mouth and then swallowing it, avoiding suspicion. And disliked for him is doing what he thinks will weaken him for fasting, like phlebotomy, cupping, and hard work, because it exposes [the fast] to corruption. And nine things are not disliked for the fasting person, and even if known by implication, it is permissible to mention them for the evidence: kissing and direct touching with security from ejaculation and intercourse, based on what is narrated from Aisha (RA) that the Prophet (SAW) used to kiss and touch while fasting, reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim; this is the apparent narration. And from Muhammad that he disliked it, and this is the narration of al-Hasan from the Imam because it does not lack temptation. In al-Jawhara, it is said that direct touching is disliked even if secure, according to the correct position. And oiling the mustache (pronounced with fatha as a verbal noun, and with damma as a substitute for the noun of action) because there is nothing in it that contradicts fasting. And kohl because the Prophet (SAW) applied kohl while fasting. And cupping that does not weaken him for fasting. And bloodletting like cupping. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that the condition for dislike is weakness requiring fragrance. And siwak is not disliked for him at the end of the day; rather it is sunnah like at the beginning, due to his saying (SAW): 'Whoever...'
Source pages p. 680

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi deals with actions that are makruh (disliked) for a fasting person and those that are not. It begins with chewing bitter almond (الكندر), which is disliked because it may break the fast by chewing, and both men and women are cautioned—Imam Ali's saying warns against actions that appear suspicious. Outside fasting, it is recommended for women but disliked for men unless in seclusion. Kissing and direct touching are disliked if there is no safety from ejaculation or intercourse, based on zahir al-riwaya. Immodest kissing (sucking the lip) is always disliked. Collecting saliva and swallowing it intentionally is disliked to avoid suspicion of breaking the fast. Actions that weaken the fasting person, like phlebotomy, cupping, and hard work, are also disliked. Then nine things are listed as not disliked, including kissing and touching with security (supported by hadith of Aisha), oiling the mustache, kohl, cupping that does not weaken, bloodletting, and siwak at the end of the day (which is sunnah). The passage includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad al-Shaybani considers kissing/touching disliked even with security, based on the risk of temptation. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the one of Abu Hanifa and the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in zahir al-riwaya) that it is not disliked with security.

Key Terms
الكندر (al-kundur): Bitter almond; chewing it is disliked because it may cause suspicion of breaking the fast.
القبلة (al-qubla): Kissing; disliked if there is no safety from ejaculation, not disliked with safety.
المباشرة (al-mubashara): Direct touching (skin-to-skin); same ruling as kissing in terms of dislike.
السواك (al-siwak): Using a tooth-stick; sunnah at any time of day for a fasting person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT disliked for a fasting person if he feels secure from ejaculation?
A) Oiling the mustache
B) Kissing
C) Collecting saliva and swallowing it
D) Cupping that weakens him

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits kissing and touching during fasting when one is secure from ejaculation, based on the prophetic example.

Kissing and Physical Contact During Fasting: Intra-Hanafi Rulings
Lists permissible acts with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kissing, oiling, kohl, cupping, bathing, and wrapping in wet cloth during fasting, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on wrapping with wet cloth.

Core Idea
Kissing and cooling actions are permissible during fasting unless they lead to desire or show irritation.

Fasting does not prohibit all physical acts; many are permissible as long as they do not break the fast or lead to forbidden outcomes. This lesson explores the Hanafi rulings on common physical acts during fasting, with special attention to the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his students on wrapping in a wet cloth to cool down.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permissible acts
From the passage, list the acts that are not disliked: kissing, oiling the mustache, applying kohl, cupping, bathing, and wrapping in a wet cloth.
2
Examine the primary evidence
For each act, note the evidence cited: hadith for kissing (Bukhari/Muslim), practice of the Prophet for kohl and pouring water, and the actions of Ibn Umar for the wet cloth.
3
Analyze the dispute on wrapping
Contrast Abu Hanifa's view (disliked due to showing irritation) with Abu Yusuf's view (permissible, mufta bihi, because it aids worship). Understand the tarjih mechanism.
4
Apply the principle of aiding worship
The Hanafi school prioritizes acts that help the fasting person endure hardship, as long as they do not break the fast or lead to sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم يقبل ويباشر ، وهو صائم رواه الشيخان وهذا ظاهر الرواية وعن محمد أنه كره وهي رواية الحسن عن الإمام لأنها لا تخلو عن فتنة، وفي الجوهرة، وقيل إنَّ المباشرة تكره، وإن أمن على الصحيح، وهي أن يمس فرجه فجرها ودهن الشارب يفتح الدال على أنه مصدر وبضمها على إقامة اسم العين مقام المصدر لأنه ليس فيه شيء ينافي الصوم (والكحل) لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام اكتحل وهو صائم ( والحجامة) التي لا تضعفه عن الصوم (والقصد كالحجامة وذكر شيخ الإسلام أنّ شرط الكراهة ضعف يحتاج فيه إلى العطر (و) لا يكره له السواك آخر النهار بل هو سنة كأوّله لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: من خير خلال الصائم السواك. وفي الكفاية كان النبي : يستاك أول النهار وآخره وهو صائم وفي الجامع الصغير للسيوطي: السواك سنة فاستاكوا أي وقت شئتم ولقوله : صلاة بسواك أفضل من سبعين صلاة بلا سواك وهي عامة لوصفها بصفة عامة تصدق بعصر الصائم كما في الفتح (و) لا يكره (لو كان رطباً) أخضر (أو) مبلولاً بالماء) لإطلاق ما روینا (و) لا يكره له (المضمضة والاستنشاق وقد فعلهما الغير وضوء و) لا (الاغتسال و) لا (التلفف بثوب مبتل قصد ذلك (للتبرد) ودفع الحر (على المفتي به) وهو قول أبي يوسف لأن النبي صب على رأسه الماء، وهو صائم من العطش، أو من الحر رواه أبو داود وكان ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما يبل الثوب، ويلفه عليه وهو صائم، ولأن بهذه عوناً على العبادة ودفعاً للضجر الطبيعي، وكرهها أبو حنيفة لما فيه من إظهار الضجر في إقامة العبادة
The book of fasting: He kisses and touches directly while fasting, narrated by the two Shaykhs [Bukhari and Muslim], and this is the apparent narration. From Muhammad it is disliked, and this is the narration of al-Hasan from the Imam because it is not free from temptation. In al-Jawhara, it is said that direct contact is disliked, even if safe, on the correct view, and it is that he touches her private part. Oiling the mustache (the word is opened in the dal as a masdar, and with dammah it is substituting the noun of the entity for the masdar) because there is nothing in it that contradicts the fast. (And kohl) because the Prophet applied kohl while fasting. (And cupping) that does not weaken him for fasting. (And cupping is like cupping), and Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that the condition for dislike is weakness that requires perfume. (And) it is not disliked for him to use a toothstick at the end of the day, rather it is sunnah like at its beginning, due to his saying: 'From the best qualities of the fasting person is the toothstick.' In al-Kifayah: The Prophet used the toothstick at the beginning and end of the day while fasting. In al-Jami' al-Saghir of al-Suyuti: The toothstick is sunnah, so use it whenever you wish. And due to his saying: 'A prayer with a toothstick is better than seventy prayers without a toothstick' and it is general because it is described with a general attribute, applying to the time of the fasting person as in al-Fath. (And) it is not disliked even if it is moist (green) or moistened with water, due to what we have narrated. (And) it is not disliked for him to rinse his mouth and snuff water, and he did them both for something other than wudu. (And) not (bathing) and not (wrapping himself in a wet cloth) intending that for cooling and repelling heat, according to the fatwa (mufta bihi) which is the saying of Abu Yusuf, because the Prophet poured water on his head while fasting due to thirst or heat, narrated by Abu Dawud, and Ibn Umar used to wet a cloth and wrap it around himself while fasting, and because it helps in worship and repels natural irritation. Abu Hanifa disliked it because it shows irritation in performing worship.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah addresses what acts are not disliked (makruh) for a fasting person. The passage type is a ruling with intra-Hanafi disputes. The text begins by discussing kissing and direct physical contact (mubasharah). According to Zahir al-Riwayah (the apparent narration from Abu Hanifa), kissing and touching are permissible for the fasting person if one is safe from desire. This is based on hadith from Bukhari and Muslim that the Prophet used to kiss while fasting. However, Imam Muhammad reports a disliked opinion from Abu Hanifa (via Hasan ibn Ziyad) because such acts may lead to temptation. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is permissible but disliked if one fears desire. Next, the text lists other acts not disliked: applying oil to the mustache (since it has no nutritive element), applying kohl (the Prophet did so while fasting), and cupping (hijama) that does not weaken the fasting person. The condition for dislike in cupping is if one becomes weak and needs perfume. Then the ruling on siwak (toothstick) is given: it is not disliked even at the end of the day; rather it is sunnah at all times. Evidence includes the hadith 'The best qualities of the fasting person is the siwak' and that prayer with siwak is superior. This is a point of difference with the Shafi'i school, which dislikes siwak after noon. The Hanafi response is that the hadith praising the change in breath of the fasting person does not prohibit siwak; rather, it highlights the virtue of fasting. Further, siwak with a fresh green twig or one moistened with water is also not disliked, as the same general proof applies. Rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and snuffing water (istinshaq) are also not disliked, even if done for non-wudu purposes. Bathing (ghusl) and wrapping in a wet cloth to cool down are permissible according to Abu Yusuf (mufta bihi), based on the Prophet's action of pouring water on his head and Ibn Umar's practice. Abu Hanifa's dislike is overridden because the intent is to aid worship, not to show irritation. The overall principle is that acts that help the fasting person endure hardship or maintain hygiene are permissible unless they directly break the fast (e.g., swallowing) or lead to forbidden acts.

Key Terms
المباشرة (al-mubāsharah): Direct physical contact, specifically kissing or touching during fasting.
الخلوف (al-khalūf): The change in breath of a fasting person due to empty stomach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on wrapping oneself in a wet cloth to cool down while fasting?
A) Disliked (makruh) according to all Hanafi scholars
B) Permissible, based on the practice of the Prophet and Ibn Umar
C) Forbidden because it shows impatience
D) Recommended (mustahab) in all cases

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds permissibility for acts that aid worship, with the mufta bihi often preferring the more lenient view when supported by practice.

Siwak and Oral Hygiene During Fasting: Hanafi vs Shafi'i View
Lists permissible acts with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on siwak during fasting, explain the cross-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school, and identify the evidence and tarjih.

Core Idea
Siwak is sunnah at all times during fasting, even after noon, based on general proofs.

Oral hygiene is encouraged in Islam, but during fasting, some scholars restrict the use of siwak (toothstick) after noon. The Hanafi school, however, permits it at any time, including the end of the day. This lesson examines the evidence and the reasoned response to the differing Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Determine the Hanafi ruling on siwak
From the passage, note that siwak is not disliked at the end of the day; it is sunnah at any time.
2
Identify the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school dislikes siwak after noon because the Prophet's breath changes; Hanafis reject this restriction.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The text cites hadith: 'From the best qualities of the fasting person is the siwak' and 'A prayer with siwak is better than seventy prayers without siwak' – general statements applicable to all times.
4
Respond to the Shafi'i objection
The idea that siwak removes the 'better than musk' breath is countered by noting that the hadith praises the breath, not the prohibition of siwak; the breath is a virtue of fasting, not a condition.
5
Apply to fresh and moist siwak
Even a green or moist siwak is not disliked because the general proofs are unconditional.

Sawm — Fasting

The Recommendation of Suhur for the Fasting Person
Lists three recommended acts for the fasting person with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of suhur (sunnah muakkadah), explain its proof from the hadith, and distinguish between recommended and obligatory acts in fasting.

Core Idea
Suhur is a blessed sunnah; missing it does not invalidate the fast.

Suhur, the pre-dawn meal before fasting, is a highly recommended practice in the Hanafi school. This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains its basis in the Prophet's command and elaborates on the spiritual benefits, while also cautioning against overeating. Understanding the precise ruling helps distinguish between what is required and what is merely recommended in the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating that suhur is one of three recommended acts (mustahab) for the fasting person. In Hanafi fiqh, mustahab is synonymous with sunnah, meaning it is highly recommended but not obligatory.
The other two acts are not fully discussed; the passage cuts off.
2
Examine the proof (dalil)
The author cites the hadith: 'تسحروا فإن في السحور بركة' (Take suhur, for in suhur there is blessing). The imperative form (تسحروا) indicates recommendation (nadb) per Hanafi usul, as there is no evidence of punishment for leaving it.
This hadith is authentic (mutawatir in meaning) and agreed upon.
3
Analyze the explanation of 'barakah'
The commentator explains that the blessing includes attainment of piety (taqwa) and increased reward. Suhur is also a time when supplications are answered, and it involves dhikr and istighfar.
The blessing is both spiritual and physical: giving energy for the day.
4
Apply the caution against excess
The text warns against eating too much suhur, as it defeats the purpose of experiencing hunger and humility. The phrase 'إخلائه عن المراد' means emptying the fast from its intended benefit (tasting bitterness of hunger).
Moderation is key; the luxurious eat excessively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ويستحب له ثلاثة أشياء السحور) لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: تسحروا فإن في السحور بركة حصول التقوى به وزيادة الثواب ولا يكثر منه لإخلائه عن المراد كما يفعل المترفهون (و) القرب منه أي أنه يقرب من الله تعالى أي من رحمته وثوابه كما أن المتطيب مقرب عندكم، أو على تقدير مضاف أي عند ملائكة الله فإنهم يدركونه شماً أطيب من ريح المسك. قوله : (حصول التقوى به خبر لمبتدأ محذوف أي والبركة حصول التقوى بالسحور، والتقوى بفتح التاء المشددة، وفتح القاف وتشديد الواو المكسورة، ولأنه إباحة في الأكل، والشرب اللذين حرما صدر الإسلام بعد النوم فشرعه يعد ناسخاً لذلك فيدل فعله على البركة، والإنتفاع للصائم ولوقوعه في الوقت الذي يستجاب فيه الدعاء أي فإذا قام وتسحر ربما يدعو بدعوات فيستجاب له ولما يقع من المتسحرين من الذكر والاستغفار والسحور بضم السين هو الأكل سحراً والمأكول يسمى سحوراً بفتح السين وفي شرح الملتقى السحور بالفتح ما يؤكل في السدس الأخير من الليل وبالضم جمع سحر . . قوله : (الإخلائه عن المراد) وهو ذوق مرارة
(And three things are recommended for him: Suhur) due to his saying: 'Eat suhur, for in suhur there is blessing: attainment of piety and increase of reward, and do not eat too much, because it empties it from the intended [benefit] as the luxurious do. (And) its proximity: meaning he draws near to Allah, i.e., to His mercy and reward, just as the perfumed one is near to you, or on an estimated additional [word] meaning in the estimation of the angels of Allah, for they perceive it by smell better than the smell of musk. His statement: 'Attainment of piety' is the predicate of an omitted subject, i.e., the blessing is the attainment of piety through suhur. And piety is with a fatha on the tashdid, fatha on the qaf, and tashdid on the waw with kasra. And because it is a permission to eat and drink which were prohibited in early Islam after sleep, so its legislation is abrogating that, thus its action indicates blessing and benefit for the fasting person, and because it occurs at the time when supplication is answered, i.e., when he wakes and eats suhur, perhaps he makes supplications and they are answered, and due to what occurs from those eating suhur of dhikr and istighfar. And suhur with a damma on the sin is the eating at dawn, and the eaten food is called suhur with fatha on the sin. In Sharh al-Multaqa: suhur with fatha is what is eaten in the last sixth of the night, and with damma it is the plural of sahar. His statement: 'its emptiness from the intended' which is tasting bitterness.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi discusses the three recommended acts (mustahab) for a fasting person, though the text only explicitly mentions suhur and then 'proximity' (likely meaning delaying suhur until near dawn). The third recommended act is probably mentioned on the next page. The focus here is on suhur (pre-dawn meal). The ruling is based on the Prophet's command: 'Take suhur, for in suhur there is blessing.' The commentator explains that the blessing includes attaining piety (taqwa) and increased reward. However, one should not eat excessively, as that defeats the purpose of experiencing hunger and humility. The word 'suhur' can be pronounced with a damma (suhūr) meaning the act of eating at dawn, or with fatha (sahara) meaning the food itself. The time for suhur is the last sixth of the night. The commentator also notes that suhur is a time when supplications are answered, and it involves dhikr and istighfar. The passage also mentions 'proximity' (al-qurb minhu), which likely refers to delaying suhur to be close to dawn, as that is the sunnah. The text is incomplete at the end, cutting off mid-explanation.

Key Terms
السحور (al-sahūr / al-suḥūr): The pre-dawn meal eaten before the fast.
البركة (al-barakah): Blessing, meaning spiritual and physical benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) of suhur in the Hanafi school according to this passage?
A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Wajib (necessary)
C) Sunnah muakkadah (emphasized recommended)
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
Suhur is a blessed sunnah that brings reward; omitting it is not sinful but deprives one of great blessing.

Permissible and Recommended Acts for the Fasting Person
Rulings on acts not disliked and recommended for the fasting person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which acts are permissible and which are recommended for a fasting person according to the Hanafi school, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding cooling with a wet garment.

Core Idea
The fasting person may use wet siwak, rinse, bathe, and cool with a wet garment; suhoor and hastening iftar are recommended.

This passage expands on what a fasting person may do beyond abstaining from food and drink. The author clarifies that certain acts often thought to be disliked—like using a moist siwak or wrapping in a wet garment—are actually permissible, and even recommended practices like suhoor are emphasised. Understanding these rulings prevents unnecessary hardship and preserves the spiritual focus of fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
List all acts discussed: siwak (moist or dry), rinsing mouth and nose, bathing, wrapping in a wet garment, suhoor, delaying suhoor, and hastening iftar. For each, determine whether the ruling is permissible (mubah), recommended (mustahab), or not disliked.
Pay attention to the phrase 'on the mufta bihi' for the wet garment.
2
Locate the proofs for each ruling
The text cites hadith for siwak (general command), for wet garment (Prophet's action), and for suhoor and iftar (explicit hadith). Find each proof and its source.
The proof for siwak is its general sunnah, not limited to non-fasting.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how each hadith yields the ruling. For example, the Prophet pouring water on his head while fasting implies permission for cooling actions.
Direct action (fi'l) of the Prophet indicates permissibility unless evidence of prohibition.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa disliked wrapping in a wet garment, viewing it as showing irritation. Abu Yusuf permitted it based on the prophetic example and because it aids worship. Identify which view is chosen as mufta bihi.
The dispute is legitimate within the school; later scholarship favoured Abu Yusuf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih mechanism
The text explicitly says 'according to the mufta bihi' and attributes it to Abu Yusuf. Understand that tarjih here relies on the strength of the dalil (Prophet's action) and the principle of aiding worship.
Also note the response to Abu Hanifa's objection: showing human weakness is not showing irritation.

بالماء قال : السحور بركة فلا تدعوه ولو أن يجرع أحدكم جرعة ماء فإن الله وملائكته يصلون على المتسحرين . رواه أحمد.
The best of the fasting person's acts is the siwak. In al-Kifaya: The Prophet used the siwak at the beginning and end of the day while fasting. In al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Suyuti: Siwak is sunnah, so use it whenever you wish, and because he said: 'A prayer with siwak is better than seventy prayers without siwak,' and it is general due to its being described with a general description, applying to the time of the fasting person as in al-Fath. (And) it is not disliked if it is moist, green, or wet with water, due to the generality of what we narrated. (And) it is not disliked for him to rinse his mouth and nose, and he did both with ablution, nor (is it disliked) to bathe, nor to wrap himself in a wet garment intending it for cooling and repelling heat according to the mufta bihi, which is the saying of Abu Yusuf, because the Prophet poured water on his head while fasting from thirst or heat, narrated by Abu Dawud, and Ibn Umar used to wet his garment and wrap it on himself while fasting, and because these are aids to worship and repelling natural irritation. Abu Hanifa disliked it because it shows irritation in performing worship. (And) three things are recommended for him: Suhoor, because the Prophet said: 'Take suhoor, for in suhoor there is blessing' – obtaining taqwa through it and increase of reward, and he should not eat much of it because it empties him from the intended (feeling of hunger) as the pampered do. (And) its delay: because he said: 'Three are from the character of the messengers: hastening iftar, delaying suhoor, and placing the right hand on the left in prayer.' And hastening iftar on a non-cloudy day; on a cloudy day one is cautious to preserve the fast from corruption, and the recommended hastening is before the stars become numerous, as mentioned by Qadikhan. Blessing is even with water: He said: 'Suhoor is blessing, so do not leave it, even if one of you takes a sip of water, for indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon those who take suhoor.' Narrated by Ahmad.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage is a legal expansion on what is permissible and recommended for a fasting person. It begins with the ruling on siwak (tooth-cleaning stick). The author states that siwak is the best act for the fasting person, citing hadith that the Prophet used it at both ends of the day while fasting. The text then clarifies that siwak is not disliked even if the stick is moist or wet with water, because general hadiths indicate its permissibility. Next, it covers rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and inhaling water (istinshaq) during ablution, bathing (ghusl), and wrapping oneself in a wet garment to cool down. According to the mufta bihi (relied upon position) of Abu Yusuf, these are not disliked, as they aid worship and relieve natural discomfort. Abu Hanifa had disliked them, thinking they show annoyance, but the response is that they show human weakness, not irritation. Then, three recommendations are listed: suhoor (pre-dawn meal), delaying suhoor until near dawn, and hastening iftar. Evidence is provided from hadith. The author explains the spiritual benefits of suhoor, including blessing and opportunities for supplication. Delaying suhoor is from the practices of prophets, and hastening iftar is recommended except on cloudy days when one should delay slightly to be sure of sunset. Even a sip of water is considered suhoor and brings blessings.

Key Terms
سواك (siwak): A stick used for cleaning teeth, often from the arak tree. In this context, it is recommended for the fasting person, even if moist.
سحور (suhoor): The pre-dawn meal taken before the fast begins. It is recommended and its delay is also recommended.
إفطار (iftar): Breaking the fast at sunset. Hastening it is recommended except on cloudy days when one should be cautious.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is makruh tahrimi.
B) It is not disliked according to the mufta bihi.
C) It is haram.
D) It is wajib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the fasting person to use a wet siwak, rinse, bathe, and cool with a wet garment, and recommends suhoor, delaying suhoor, and hastening iftar.

Excuses (ʿAwāriḍ) for Not Fasting: Permission vs. Breaking
Definition and scope of excuses (ʿawāriḍ) for fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the types of ʿawāriḍ (excuses) in Hanafi fiqh and explain why they permit not starting the fast but do not allow breaking it once begun.

Core Idea
ʿAwāriḍ permit delaying the fast, not breaking it after starting.

This passage begins a new chapter on the excuses that can affect the obligation to fast. The author lists several conditions—illness, travel, compulsion, etc.—and immediately makes a critical distinction: these excuses allow one to avoid starting the fast, but once a fast has been validly begun, the same excuses do not permit breaking it. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rulings of Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its scope
The passage defines 'āriḍ (pl. ʿawāriḍ) as something that faces you—a contingency. Recognize that the chapter covers conditions like illness, travel, compulsion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, severe hunger/thirst, and old age.
Not all conditions are equally severe; later chapters detail each one.
2
Extract the core legal ruling
The key ruling: these excuses do not permit breaking an ongoing fast. Rather, they permit not starting the fast in the first place. The text uses the example of travel to prove this: if a resident starts the fast and then travels, he cannot break it.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position; other schools may differ.
3
Understand the rationale
The ruling is based on consistency: if travel permitted breaking, then anyone who started as a resident and later traveled could break, which is not allowed. Therefore, permission is limited to the start of the day.
The same logic applies to illness: a sudden illness after starting does not justify breaking.
4
Apply the distinction to a new scenario
Consider a woman who begins fasting while healthy, then falls severely ill at noon. According to this ruling, she must continue fasting; she cannot break it. She should have not started if she anticipated illness.
If the illness makes fasting harmful, there may be exceptions based on necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض جمع عارض المرض والسفر والإكراه، والحبل والرضاع والجوع والعطش والهرم بعض الجوع ليرحم المساكين، وليكون أجره على قدر مشقته. قوله : (كما يفعله المترفهون) أي المتنعمون قوله : (تأخير السحور) ويكره تأخيره إلى وقت يقع فيه الشك هندية. قوله : (وتعجيل الفطر) ويستحب الإفطار قبل الصلاة وفي البحر التعجيل المستحب التعجيل قبل اشتباك النجوم، ومن السنة عند الإفطار أن يقول اللهم لك صمت وبك آمنت، وعليك توكلت وعلى رزقك أفطرت، وصوم الغد من شهر رمضان نويت فاغفر لي ما قدمت، وما أخرت. قوله : (قبل استفحال النجوم أي ظهورها وتبين كل نجم ،بانفراده، وهو بالفاء، والحاء المهملة ويقال لسهيل : فحل لاعتزاله النجوم كالفحل فإنه إذا قرع الإبل اعتزالها أفاده في القاموس. قوله : (ولو) أن يجرع أحدكم جرعة ماء قال في القاموس : الجرعة مثلثة . حسوة منه، أو بالفتح وبالضم الإسم من جرع الماء كسمع، ومنع بلعه، وبالضم ما اجترعت قوله : ( يصلون على المتسحرين أي الله يرحم والملائكة تستغفر لهم، أو يراد بها العطف، وهو في كل بما يناسبه، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم. اهـ. فصل: في العوارض من الماء هي جديرة بالتأخير جمع عارض، وهو كل ما استقبلك، ومنه عارض ممطرنا وهو السحاب، والعارض الباب والخد وعرض له عارض أي آفة من كبر أو، مرض كذا في ضياء الحلوم، ولما كان إفساد الصوم بغير عذر يوجب إثماً، وبعذر لا يوجبه احتيج إلى بيان الأعذار المسقطة له نهر قوله (والسفر) فيه أنه لا يبيح الفطر، وإنما يبيح عدم الشروع في الصوم إذ لو كان السفر يبيح الفطر لجاز لمن أصبح مقيماً، ثم سافر الفطر مع أنه لا يجوز، وحينئذ فالمراد بالعوارض هنا ما يبيح عدم الصوم ليطرد في الكل أفاده السيد، وكذا يراد بالفطر في قوله بها يباح الفطر ما أباح عدم الصوم سواء أباحه من أوله أو بعد الشروع فيه .
Chapter on the 'awarid (excuses): Plural of 'arid: illness, travel, compulsion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and senility. Some hunger to show mercy to the poor, and so that his reward is according to his effort. ... (The text continues with commentary on previous points and then the new fasl.) Chapter: On the 'awarid: They are deserving of delay. Plural of 'arid, which is everything that faces you, including a cloud that rains on us, and the 'arid is the door and the cheek, and an 'arid afflicts him, meaning an affliction of old age or illness. In Nihr: And travel does not permit breaking the fast, but rather permits not starting the fast; for if travel permitted breaking, it would be permissible for one who became a resident then traveled to break, which is not allowed. Therefore, by 'awarid here is meant what permits not fasting, to be consistent for all. Likewise, by 'iftar' in the statement 'it permits iftar' is meant what permits abstaining from fasting, whether from the beginning or after starting.
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on 'awarid (excuses) that affect the obligation of fasting. The term 'arid literally means something that faces one, and here refers to conditions that may excuse one from fasting. The author lists illness, travel, compulsion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, severe hunger, thirst, and old age. A key distinction is made: these excuses do not permit breaking a fast once started, but rather they permit not starting the fast in the first place. For example, if a person begins the fast while a resident and then travels, they may not break the fast because the journey did not precede the intention. The chapter explains the legal rulings for each excuse, emphasizing that the purpose is to clarify which conditions lift the obligation to fast or allow postponement. The passage also includes some linguistic notes on the word 'arid.

Key Terms
عارض ('āriḍ (pl. ʿawāriḍ)): An excuse or contingency that arises and may affect the obligation to fast, such as illness or travel.
سفر (safar (travel)): A journey that qualifies as an excuse; it permits not starting the fast but does not allow breaking it once started.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must break the fast.
B) He may break the fast and make it up later.
C) He is not permitted to break the fast because the excuse of travel only applies before starting.
D) He may continue or break as he wishes.

Key Takeaway
Whether an excuse permits not starting or breaking the fast depends on timing: permission is only for the commencement, not the continuation of the fast.

Recommended Timing for Suhur and Iftar
Indicates recommended practices for suhur and iftar with prophetic hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended timings for suhur and iftar in the Hanafi school and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Suhur should be delayed, iftar hastened – these are sunnah practices of the messengers.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the recommended times for the pre-dawn meal (suhur) and breaking the fast (iftar) in Ramadan. It cites a hadith listing three prophetic traits, emphasizing barakah in suhur and the virtue of hastening iftar. Understanding these rulings helps you follow the sunnah in daily fasting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
Determine what action is being ruled upon and the hukm level. Here, suhur (delaying) and iftar (hastening) are recommended (sunnah muakkadah).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof: a hadith from Musnad Ahmad stating three traits of messengers, including hastening iftar and delaying suhur.
Hadith is ahad, thus zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith indicates recommendation: the words 'تعجيل' and 'تأخير' imply desirability, and the context of 'أخلاق المرسلين' shows it is praiseworthy.
Imperative form here indicates sunnah, not obligation.
4
Note conditions and exceptions
Hasten iftar on clear days; on cloudy days, take precaution to ensure sunset. Delay suhur but not to the point of doubt.
From the commentary: 'استفحال النجوم' is the benchmark for recommended haste.
5
Identify the tarjih
This ruling is mutaffaq alayh (agreed upon) among schools; no tarjih needed. The practice is consistent in Zahir al-Riwayah.
Mufta bihi: delaying suhur and hastening iftar are sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستحب (تأخيره) لقوله : ثلاث من أخلاق المرسلين تعجيل الإفطار، وتأخير السحور ووضع اليمين على الشمال في الصلاة وتعجيل الفطر من غير يوم غيم) وفي الغيم يحتاط حفظاً للصوم عن الإفساد والتعجيل المستحب قبل استفحال النجوم ذكره قاضيخان، والبركة ولو بالماء قال : السحور بركة فلا تدعوه ولو أن يجرع أحدكم جرعة ماء فإن الله وملائكته يصلون على المتسحرين . رواه أحمد.
فصل في العوارض
جمع عارض المرض والسفر والإكراه، والحبل والرضاع والجوع والعطش والهرم
بعض الجوع ليرحم المساكين، وليكون أجره على قدر مشقته. قوله : (كما يفعله المترفهون) أي المتنعمون قوله : (تأخير السحور) ويكره تأخيره إلى وقت يقع فيه الشك هندية. قوله : (وتعجيل الفطر) ويستحب الإفطار قبل الصلاة وفي البحر التعجيل المستحب التعجيل قبل اشتباك النجوم، ومن السنة عند الإفطار أن يقول اللهم لك صمت وبك آمنت، وعليك توكلت وعلى رزقك أفطرت، وصوم الغد من شهر رمضان نويت فاغفر لي ما قدمت، وما أخرت. قوله : (قبل استفحال النجوم أي ظهورها وتبين كل نجم ،بانفراده، وهو بالفاء، والحاء المهملة ويقال لسهيل : فحل لاعتزاله النجوم كالفحل فإنه إذا قرع الإبل اعتزالها أفاده في القاموس. قوله : (ولو) أن يجرع أحدكم جرعة ماء قال في القاموس : الجرعة مثلثة . حسوة منه، أو بالفتح وبالضم الإسم من جرع الماء كسمع، ومنع بلعه، وبالضم ما اجترعت قوله : ( يصلون على المتسحرين أي الله يرحم والملائكة تستغفر لهم، أو يراد بها العطف، وهو في كل بما يناسبه، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم.
اهـ.
It is recommended to delay it because of his statement: 'Three things are from the character of the messengers: hastening the iftar, delaying the suhur, placing the right hand over the left in prayer, and hastening the iftar on a non-cloudy day.' And on a cloudy day, one takes precaution to safeguard the fast from invalidation. The recommended haste is before the full appearance of the stars, as mentioned by Qadikhan. And there is barakah even with water. He said: 'Suhur has barakah, so do not abandon it, even if one of you takes a sip of water, for indeed Allah and His angels send blessings upon those who take suhur.' Narrated by Ahmad.
Section on Impediments
Collective term for the impediment: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age. ... Some hunger to have mercy on the poor, and so that his reward is proportional to his hardship. His statement: (as the luxury-seekers do) meaning the pleasure-seekers. His statement: (delaying suhur) and it is disliked to delay it to a time of doubt, according to Hindiyyah. His statement: (and hastening iftar) and it is recommended to break the fast before prayer. In al-Bahr, the recommended haste is before the intermingling of stars. Among the sunnah at iftar is to say: 'O Allah, for You I fasted, and in You I believed, and upon You I relied, and on Your provision I break my fast; and for the fast of tomorrow from the month of Ramadan I intend, so forgive me what I have sent ahead and what I have delayed.' His statement: (before the full appearance of the stars) meaning their appearance and clear manifestation of each star individually; it is with fa and unpointed ha; and it is said for Suhayl: fahl because he separates himself from the stars like a male camel separates itself from camels when it mounts them. This is from al-Qamus. His statement: (even if) one of you takes a sip of water. He said in al-Qamus: al-jur'ah is a triple voweled word; it means a gulp; or with fatha and with damm it is the noun from jar'a al-ma'a (to sip), and with damm it is what one sips. His statement: (send blessings upon those taking suhur) meaning Allah has mercy and the angels ask forgiveness for them, or it is understood as relational grace appropriate to each; and Allah most high knows best, and I seek forgiveness of Allah, the Mighty. End.
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the recommended timing for suhur and iftar, based on a hadith listing three prophetic traits: hastening iftar, delaying suhur, and placing the right hand on the left in prayer. The hastening of iftar is recommended on clear days; on cloudy days one may delay slightly to be certain of sunset. The delay of suhur is recommended until late, but not so late that doubt arises. The commentary elaborates on the wording, explaining that the recommended haste for iftar is before the stars fully appear. The hadith encourages suhur even with a sip of water, and mentions that Allah and His angels send blessings upon those who take suhur. The section then begins a new topic: 'Impediments' (al-'awarid), listing illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age. The commentary also notes that some interpret 'joys of the rich' as abstaining from food for a greater reward. The passage concludes with the wording of the supplication at iftar and linguistic clarifications on stars and sips.

Key Terms
السحور (al-suhūr): The pre-dawn meal taken before the fast begins.
الإفطار (al-ifṭār): Breaking the fast at sunset.
استفحال النجوم (istifḥāl al-nujūm): The full appearance and clear manifestation of each star individually.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on taking suhur even if only a sip of water?
A) It is obligatory.
B) It is recommended and brings barakah.
C) It is makruh to take less than a full meal.
D) It is permissible but not recommended.

Key Takeaway
Suhur should be delayed and iftar hastened as sunnah, based on prophetic hadith, with barakah even from a sip of water.
